<TABLE>
<TR><TD COLSPAN=5><a name="description"></a>&nbsp;<p><p align='center'><strong> 2.A.6  The Resistance-Nodulation-Cell Division (RND) Superfamily</strong></p>
<p>Characterized members of the RND superfamily all probably catalyze substrate efflux via an H<sup>+</sup> antiport mechanism.These proteins are found ubiquitously in bacteria, archaea and eukaryotes (see <span id='ref34548312.29577985' class='cite'>Nikaido 2018</span> for a review of RND transporters).  They fall into ten recognized phylogenetic families, three primary phylogenetic families that are restricted largely to Gram-negative bacteria (families 1-3, see below), the SecDF family (family  4) that is represented in both Gram-negative and Gram-positive bacteria as well as archaea, the HAE2 family (family 5) that is restricted to Gram-positive bacteria, one very diverse eukaryotic family (family 6), one archaeal plus spirochete family (family 7) (<span id='ref1232.10941792' class='cite'>Tseng <em>et al</em>., 1999</span>), a recently identified family that includes a probable pigment exporter in Gram-negative bacteria (TC #2.A.6.8.1; <span id='ref2160.12057948' class='cite'>Goel <em>et al</em>., 2002</span>), Dispatched (family 9), an exporter of the amino terminal portion (19 kDa) of Hedgehog which has a C-terminal cholesterol covanlent linkage that may be recognized by Dispatched (<span id='ref34548312.29577985' class='cite'>Nikaido 2018</span>), and the uncharacterized family 10 from Actinobacteria. For mechanistic considerations, see <span id='ref34552037.32936941' class='cite'>Murakami <em>et al.</em> 2020</span> and <span id='ref34552041.32960482' class='cite'>Simsir <em>et al.</em> 2020</span>.</p>
<p>Clustering patterns in the Gram-negative bacterial families of the RND superfamily correlate with substrate specificity with family 1 catalyzing export of heavy metals, family 2 catalyzing export of multiple drugs, cluster 3 probably catalyzing export of lipooligosaccharides concerned with plant nodulation for the purpose of symbiotic nitrogen fixation, cluster 4 corresponding to the SecDF proteins, and cluster 8 catalyzing pigment export.  Within family 2, MdtABC, consisting of an MFP (TC #8.A.1) and two RND family proteins (MdtB [TC #2.A.6.2.12] and MdtC [TC #2.A.6.2.14]) may form a complex exhibiting broader specificity than either MdtAB or MdtAC (<span id='ref2159.12107134' class='cite'>Baranova and Nikaido, 2002</span>; <span id='ref2161.12107133' class='cite'>Nagakubo <em>et al</em>., 2002</span>).  The ActII3 protein, one of the two partially characterized member of family 5, has been implicated in drug resistance.  The MmpL7 protein, also of this family, catalyzes export of an outer membrane lipid, phthiocerol dimycocerosate (PDIM) in <em>M. tuberculosis.</em> The SecDF proteins (family 4) function as nonessential constituents of the IISP protein secretory system (TC #3.A.5).  They seem to allow coupling of substrate protein translocation to the proton motive force by facilitating deinsertion of the SecA component of the IISP system, thus rendering this system partially ATP-independent.</p>
<p><span style='white-space: pre;'> </span>Nies (<span id='ref9180.12829273' class='cite'>2003</span>) subdivied the HME-RND proteins (2.A.6.1) into subgroups according to substrate specificity.&nbsp; HME1 (Zn<sup>2+</sup>, Co<sup>2+</sup>, Cd<sup>2+</sup>), HME2 (Co<sup>2+</sup>, Ni<sup>2+</sup>), HME3a (divalent cations), HME3b (monovalent cations) HME4 (Cu<sup>+</sup> or Ag<sup>+</sup>) and HME5 (Ni<sup>2+</sup>).&nbsp; Kim <em>et al.</em> (<span id='ref7715.21398536' class='cite'>2011</span>) have proposed two models for the extrusion of heavy metals (2.A.6.1) from the periplasm to the extracellular medium, the 'switch' and the 'funnel' mechanisms.&nbsp;The funnel model involves the shuttling of periplasmic substrate from the membrane fusion protein to the RND transporter and further on through the outer membrane factor to the extracellular space. Conversely, the switch model requires substrate binding to the membrane fusion protein, inducing a conformational change and creating an open-access state of the tripartite protein complex. They favor the switch mechanism.</p>
<p>Some or all of the eukaryotic proteins (family 6) may function in cholesterol/lipid/steroid hormone transport, reception, regulation or catalysis.  One such protein complex includes the RND family disease protein, Niemann-Pick C1, which may function in the export of cholesterol and lipids from lysosomes in conjunction with a soluble lysosomal protein with cholesterol binding properties, NPC2 (TC #2.A.6.6.1; <span id='ref2875.15071184' class='cite'>Sleat <em>et al</em>., 2004</span>). The disorder is typified by inhibited egress of cholesterol and glycosphingolipids from endosomal and lysosomal compartments. In the majority of NPC patients, mutations in the NPC1 gene can be identified, but about 5% of patients show mutations in the NPC2 gene. Many different mutations can cause NPC disease, and multiple variants not associated with the disease are known in both genes. There is an NPC disease gene variation database (NPC-db; http://npc.fzk.de).  Non-transporter homologues possess the sterol recognition domain and do not exhibit the typical RND family internal duplication (see below).  The functions of the archaeal and spirochete proteins of family 7 have not been investigated.</p>
<p>Water-soluble Niemann-Pick C2 (NPC2) and membrane-bound NPC1 are 
cholesterol-binding lysosomal proteins required for export of 
lipoprotein-derived cholesterol from lysosomes. The binding site in NPC1
 is located in its N-terminal domain (NTD), which projects into the 
lysosomal lumen. Transfer of cholesterol from NPC2 to NPC1 requires three residues that form a patch on the
 surface of NPC2. Wang et al. (<span id='ref7341.20674861' class='cite'>2010</span>) previously identified a patch of residues on the 
surface of NPC1(NTD) that is required for transfer. They presented a model 
in which these two surface patches on NPC2 and NPC1(NTD) interact, 
thereby opening an entry pore on NPC1(NTD) and allowing cholesterol to 
transfer without passing through the water phase. They referred to this 
transfer as a hydrophobic handoff and hypothesized that this handoff is 
essential for cholesterol export from lysosomes (<span id='ref7341.20674861' class='cite'>Wang <em>et al.</em>, 2010</span>).</p>
<p>Most of the RND superfamily transport systems consist of large polypeptide chains (700-1300 amino acyl residues long).   These proteins possess a single transmembrane spanner (TMS) at their N-termini followed by a large extracytoplasmic domain, then six additional TMSs, a second large extracytoplasmic domain, and five final C-terminal TMSs.  In the case of one system (NolGHI) the system may consist of three distinct polypeptide chains, and most of the SecDF homologues consist of two polypeptide chains.  Most others probably consist of a single polypeptide chain.  The first halves of RND family proteins are homologous to the second halves, and the proteins therefore probably arose as a result of an intragenic tandem duplication event that occurred in the primordial system prior to divergence of the family members.  One protein homologue from <em>Methanococcus jannaschii</em> is of half size and has no internal duplication.  It can be postulated to function as a homo- or heterodimer in the membrane.  The same is true of the eukaryotic RND family homologues that do not appear to function in transport.  Some of the eukaryotic proteins have hydrophilic C-terminal domains.</p>
<p>Crystal structures of the RND drug exporter of <em>E. coli</em>, AcrB (TC #2.A.6.2.2), have been solved at 3.5 &Aring; and 2.8 &Aring; resolution (<span id='ref2221.12374972' class='cite'>Murakami <em>et al</em>., 2002</span>, <span id='ref3641.16915237' class='cite'>2006</span>).  Three AcrB protomers are organized as a homotrimer in the shape of a jellyfish.  Each protomer consists of a 50 &Aring; thick transmembrane domain and a 70 &Aring; headpiece, protruding from the external membrane surface.  The top of the headpiece opens like a funnel, and this may be a site of interaction with the MFP, AcrA (TC #8.A.1.6.1) and the OMF, TolC (TC #1.B.17.1.1).  A pore formed by the three &alpha;-helices connects the funnel with a central cavity at the bottom of the headpiece.  The 12 TMSs in the membrane domain are visible.  Substrates are presumably successively transported through the channels of AcrB and TolC (<span id='ref2221.12374972' class='cite'>Murakami <em>et al</em>., 2002</span>).  An MFP such as MexF of <em>P. aeruginosa</em> facilitates proper assembly of the RND permease as well as stabilization of the OMF such as OprN (<span id='ref2328.12410825' class='cite'>Maseda <em>et al</em>., 2002</span>). Vestibules are part of substrate path in AcrB multidrug efflux transporter of <em>Escherichia coli</em> (<span id='ref7913.21856849' class='cite'>Husain <em>et al.</em>, 2011</span>).&nbsp;Pag&egrave;s et al. (<span id='ref7917.21651484' class='cite'>2011</span>) have described several classes of efflux pump inhibitors that counteract MDR.</p>
<p>The large external cavity is of 5000 cubic angstroms.  Several different hydrophobic and amphipathic ligands can bind in different positions within the cavity simultaneously.  Binding involves hydrophobic forces, aromatic (&pi;) stacking and van der Waals interactions (Yu et al., 2003).  Crystallographic studies of the asymmetric trimer of AcrB suggest that each protomer in the trimeric assembly goes through a cycle of conformational changes during drug export.  The external large cleft in the periplasmic domain of AcrB appears to be closed in the crystal structure of one of the three protomers.  Conformational changes, including the closure of the external cleft in the periplasmic domain, are apparently required for drug transport by AcrB (<span id='ref4692.' class='cite'>Takatsuka and Nikaido, 2007</span>; <span id='ref6989.20212112' class='cite'>Takatsuka <em>et al.</em>, 2010</span>).</p>
<p>Murakami et al. (<span id='ref3641.16915237' class='cite'>2006</span>) have described crystal structures of AcrB with and without substrates. The AcrB-drug complex consists of three protomers, each of which has a different conformation corresponding to one of the three functional states of the transport cycle. Bound substrate was found in the periplasmic domain of one of the three protomers. The voluminous binding pocket is aromatic and allows multi-site binding. The structures indicate that drugs are exported by a three-step functionally rotating mechanism in which substrates undergo ordered binding change.  A crystal structure at 2.9 &Aring; resolution of trimeric AcrB was reported by Seeger et al. (<span id='ref3540.16946072' class='cite'>2006</span>) and shows asymmetry of the monomers.  This structure reveals three different monomer conformations representing consecutive states in a transport cycle.  The structural data imply an alternating access mechanism and a novel peristaltic mode of drug transport by this type of transporter.</p>
<p>The RND members of families 1-3 function in conjunction with a 'membrane fusion protein' (MFP; TC #8.A.1) and an 'outer membrane factor' (OMF; TC #1.B.17) to effect efflux across both membranes of the Gram-negative bacterial cell envelope in a single energy-coupled step.  They may also pump hydrophobic substances from the cytoplasmic membrane, and toxic hydrophilic substances (i.e., heavy metals) from the periplasm to the external medium.  The large periplasmic domains of RND pumps are involved in substrate recognition and form a cavity that can accommodate multiple drugs simultaneously (<span id='ref2327.12410844' class='cite'>Mao <em>et al</em>., 2002</span>). A comprehensive review of the classes of efflux pump inhibitors from 
various sources, highlighting their structure-activity relationships, 
which can be useful for medicinal chemists in the pursuit of novel 
efflux pump inhibitors, has appeared (<a class='reflink' href='../../search/result.php?tc=3.A.1#ref34549604'>Dur&atilde;es <em>et al.</em> 2018</a>). Pyridylpiperazine-based allosteric inhibitors of RND-type multidrug efflux pumps have been studied (<span id='ref34554332.35013254' class='cite'>Pl&eacute; <em>et al.</em> 2022</span>).</p>
<p><span id='ref6470.19342493' class='cite'>Symmons <em>et al.</em>, 2009</span> showed that the adaptor termini assemble a beta-roll structure forming the
final domain adjacent to the inner membrane. The completed structure
enabled in vivo cross-linking to map intermolecular contacts between
the adaptor AcrA and the transporter AcrB, defining a periplasmic
interface between several transporter subdomains and the contiguous
beta-roll, beta-barrel, and lipoyl domains of the adaptor. The flexible
linear topology of the adaptor allowed a multidomain docking approach
to model the transporter-adaptor complex, revealing that the adaptor
docks to a transporter region of comparative stability distinct from
those key to the proposed rotatory pump mechanism, putative
drug-binding pockets, and the binding site of inhibitory DARPins.
AcrA(3)-AcrB(3)-TolC(3) is a
610 KDa, 270-A-long efflux pump crossing the entire bacterial cell
envelope (<span id='ref6470.19342493' class='cite'>Symmons <em>et al.</em>, 2009</span>).</p>
<p>RND transporters such as AcrD of <em>E. coli</em> can capture drugs such as aminoglycosides, from the periplasm and maybe from the cytoplasm (<span id='ref3014.15743938' class='cite'>Aires and Nikaido, 2005</span>).  The latter process has been referred to as periplasmic vacuuming where, in this case, AcrD is the periplasmic vacuum cleaner (<span id='ref3015.15743933' class='cite'>Lomovskaya and Totrov, 2005</span>).  This allows Gram-negative bacteria to protect themselves against cell wall biosynthetic inhibitors (drugs) that act in the periplasm.  It also explains why HAE1 family members are largely restricted to Gram-negative bacteria.  They are rarely found  in Gram-positive bacteria or archaea.</p>
<p>A novel member of the RND superfamily, very distantly related to other established members of the superfamily, was shown to be a pigment (xanthomonadin) exporter in <em>Xanthomonas oryzae</em> (<span id='ref2160.12057948' class='cite'>Goel <em>et al</em>., 2002</span>).  This protein (TC #2.A.6.8.1) has close homologues in various species of <em>Xanthomonas</em> as well as <em>Xylella, Ralstonia</em> and <em>E. coli</em> (AAG58596).  These proteins comprise the eighth recognized family in the RND superfamily.</p>
<p>Protein translocation across the bacterial membrane, mediated by the 
secretory translocon SecYEG and the SecA ATPase, is enhanced by the proton 
motive force and membrane-integrated SecDF, which associates with 
SecYEG. The role of SecDF has been shown
to function in late stages of protein secretion and membrane 
protein biogenesis.&nbsp;<span id='ref10120.21562494' class='cite'>Tsukazaki <em>et al.</em> (2011)</span> determined the crystal structure of <em>Thermus
 thermophilus</em> SecDF TC# 2.A.6.4.3) at 3.3&thinsp;&Aring; resolution, revealing a pseudo-symmetrical,
 12-helix transmembrane domain belonging to the RND superfamily and two 
major periplasmic domains, P1 and P4. Higher-resolution analysis of the 
periplasmic domains suggested that P1, which binds an unfolded protein, 
undergoes functionally important conformational changes. <em>In vitro </em>analyses identified an ATP-independent step of protein translocation 
that requires both SecDF and the proton motive force. Electrophysiological 
analyses revealed that SecDF conducts protons in a manner dependent on 
pH and the presence of an unfolded protein, with conserved Asp and Arg 
residues at the transmembrane interface between SecD and SecF playing 
essential roles in the movements of protons and preproteins. Therefore, 
<span id='ref10120.21562494' class='cite'>Tsukazaki <em>et al.</em> (2011)</span> proposed that SecDF functions as a membrane-integrated chaperone, 
powered by the proton motive force, to achieve ATP-independent protein 
translocation. <span id='ref34549431.29398525' class='cite'>Furukawa <em>et al.</em> 2018</span>&nbsp; reported the crystal structure of SecDF in a form named the super-membrane-facing form, demonstrating a beta-barrel architecture instead of the previously reported beta-sheet structure. A remote coupling model was proposed in which a structural change of the transmembrane region drives a functional, extracytoplasmic conformational transition.</p>
<p>AcrB actively exports a wide variety of noxious compounds using the 
proton-motive force for energy. AcrB 
adopts an asymmetric structure of three protomers with different
 conformations that are sequentially converted during drug export; these
 cyclic conformational changes during drug export are referred to as 
functional rotation. Using different protonation states for the titratable 
residues in the middle of the transmembrane domain, simulations 
revealed a correlation between the specific protonation states and the
 side-chain configurations. Changing the protonation state for Asp408 
induced a spontaneous structural transition, which suggests that the 
proton translocation stoichiometry may be one proton per functional 
rotation cycle.&nbsp; Simulations also demonstrate that 
alternating the protonation states in the transmembrane domain induces 
functional rotation in the porter domain, which is coupled to drug transport (<span id='ref10411.24083838' class='cite'>Yamane <em>et al.</em> 2013</span>).&nbsp; A mechanism involving two remote alternating-access conformational cycles within each protomer, namely one for
protons in the transmembrane region and another for drugs in the periplasmic domain, 50 A apart, has been proposed (<span id='ref34544432.25248080' class='cite'>Eicher <em>et al.</em> 2014</span>).
Each of these cycles entails two distinct types of collective motions of two structural repeats,
coupled by flanking &alpha;-helices that project from the membrane. Cross-talk among protomers across the trimerization interface might lead to a more kinetically
efficient efflux system.</p>
<p>The generalized transport reaction catalyzed by functionally characterized RND proteins is:</p>
<p align='center'>Substrates (in) + nH<sup>+</sup> (out)  &rarr;  Substrates (out) + nH<sup>+</sup> (in).</p>
<p>Substrates: (a) heavy metals, (e.g., Co<sup>2+</sup>, Zn<sup>2+</sup>, Cd<sup>2+</sup>, Ni<sup>2+</sup>, Cu<sup>+</sup> and Ag<sup>+</sup>; family 1); (b) multiple drugs (e.g., tetracycline, chloramphenicol, fluoroquinolones, &beta;-lactams, etc.; family 2); (c) lipooligosaccharides (nodulation factors; family 3); (d) unfolded proteins as for the SecDF-mediated translocation of substrate proteins (Family 4), (e) lipids and possibly antibiotic drugs in Gram positive bacteria (e.g., outer membrane mycolic acid-containing lipids in actinobacteria and&nbsp; actinorhodin; family 5), (f) possibly sterols in eukaryotes (family 6), (g) fused pentacyclic ring compounds such a hopanoids in bacteria (family 7), (h) pigments (family 8), and (i) cholesterol-modified peptides such as 'hedgehog', a sterol sensor in animals (family 9).</p><BR></TD></TR><TR><TD COLSPAN=5><HR>This family belongs to the <A HREF="superfamily.php?id=53">Resistance-Nodulation-Cell Division (RND) Superfamily</A>.<BR><HR></TD></TR><TR><TD COLSPAN=5><a name="references"></a>&nbsp;<p><B>References:</B><P></TD></TR><TR><TD COLSPAN=5><BR><HR></TD></TR><TR><TD COLSPAN=5> and Rayasam GV. editors, Caister Acadmic Press, 2013).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Proteobacteria</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>NimA of <em>Cupriavidus metallidurans</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="2.A.6.1.2"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=2.A.6.1.2&tcname=">2.A.6.1.2</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p><a></a></p>
<p><!-- test--></p>
<p><a id="2.A.6.1.2"></a></p>
<p>Heavy metal (Co<sup>2+</sup><sup>,</sup>&nbsp;Zn<sup>2+</sup>, Cd<sup>2+</sup>) efflux pump, CzcAB.&nbsp; Functions with CzcC (P13509; 1.B.17.2.2).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Gram-negative bacteria</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>CzcA/CzcB of <em>Cupriavidus (Ralstonia; Alcaligenes) metallidurans (eutriphus or eutropha)</em><br />CzcA (P13511)<br />CzcB (P13510)&nbsp;</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="2.A.6.1.3"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=2.A.6.1.3&tcname=">2.A.6.1.3</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC>Silver ion (Ag<sup>+</sup>)-specific efflux pump </TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC>Gram-negative bacteria </TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC>SilA of <I>Salmonella typhimurium</i> </TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="2.A.6.1.4"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=2.A.6.1.4&tcname=">2.A.6.1.4</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p><a></a></p>
<p><!-- test--></p>
<p><a id="2.A.6.1.4"></a></p>
<p>Cu<sup>+</sup><sup> </sup>/Ag<sup>+</sup><sup> </sup> efflux pump, CusABCF (may pump ions from the periplasm to the external medium); CusF is a periplasmic Cu<sup>+</sup><sup> </sup>/Ag<sup>+</sup><sup> </sup> binding receptor essential for full resistance (<a class="reflink" href="result.php?tc=2.A.6#ref2536">Franke <em>et al</em>., 2003</a>). Bagai et al. (<a class="reflink" href="result.php?tc=2.A.6#ref4779">2007</a>) reported that CusB (MFP) binds one molecule of Ag<sup>+</sup><sup> </sup> or Cu<sup>+</sup><sup> </sup> via four conserved methionines and induces a substrate-linked conformational change (<a class="reflink" href="result.php?tc=2.A.6#ref4779">Bagai <em>et al</em>., 2007</a>). The crystal structures of CusB are available (<a class="reflink" href="result.php?tc=2.A.6#ref6765">Su <em>et al.</em>, 2009</a>). The crystal structure of the CusAB complex has been solved (PDB# 3K07) (<span id="ref7636.21350490" class="cite">Su <em>et al.</em>, 2011a</span>). CusC is listed under TC# 1.B.17.3.5. The metal-binding methionines play a role in restricting the substrates to monovalent heavy metals (<a class="reflink" href="result.php?tc=2.A.6#ref7096">Conroy <em>et al.</em>, 2010</a>). It has been reported to export L-cysteine (<a class="reflink" href="result.php?tc=2.A.6#ref7184">Yamada <em>et al.</em>, 2006</a>). Crystal structures of the CusA efflux pump suggested that methionine residues in a 3-methionine cluster, bind the metal as a transport intermediate (<a class="reflink" href="result.php?tc=2.A.6#ref7281">Long <em>et al.</em>, 2010</a>). Four methionine pairs in the transmembrane region, and one in the periplasmic domain may comprise the channel. Cu<sup>+</sup><sup> </sup> is exported from the cytoplasm to the periplasmic chaparone, CusF in the extracellular space (<span id="ref34543572.24917681" class="cite">Padilla-Benavides <em>et al.</em> 2014</span>). The Cus efflux system removes Cu<sup>+</sup> and Ag<sup>+</sup> from both the cell cytoplasm and the periplasm (<span id="ref7470.20981744" class="cite">Su <em>et al.</em>, 2011b</span>; <span id="ref11327.24702006" class="cite">Delmar <em>et al.</em> 2014</span>). Metal-bound CusB is required for activation of Cu<sup>+</sup> transfer from
 CusF directly to a site in the CusA antiporter (<span id="ref34543552.25313055" class="cite">Chac&oacute;n <em>et al.</em> 2014</span>). Metal transfer occurs between 
CusF and apo-CusB, and when metal-loaded, CusB plays a role in the
 regulation of metal ion transfer from CusF to CusA in the periplasm.&nbsp; The ratio of CusA (RND):CusB (MFP):CusC (OMF) is 3:6:3 (<span id="ref34543664.23657864" class="cite">Delmar <em>et al.</em> 2013</span>). Intermediates in metal transfer reactions have been measured (<span id="ref34549086.30456313" class="cite">Chac&oacute;n <em>et al.</em> 2018</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Gram-negative bacteria</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>CusCFBA of <em>E. coli</em>: <br /> CusA (RND) <br /> CusB (MFP) <br /> CusC (OMF) (see 1.B.17.3.5)<br /> CusF (BP)</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="2.A.6.1.5"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=2.A.6.1.5&tcname=">2.A.6.1.5</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC>The Zn<sup>2+</sup>, Cd<sup>2+</sup>, Pb<sup>2+</sup> exporter, CzcCBA1 (induced by Zn<sup>2+</sup>, Cd<sup>2+</sup>, Pb<sup>2+</sup>, Ni<sup>2+</sup>, Co<sup>2+</sup> and Hg<sup>2+</sup> (<span id="ref5079.18065533" class="cite">Leedjarv <em>et al</em>., 2007</span>))</TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC>Bacteria</TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC>CzcCBA1 of <i>Pseudomonas putida</i><br>
CzcA1 (RND) (Q88RT6)<br>
CzcB1 (MFP) (Q88RT5)<br>
CzcC1 (OMF) (Q88RT4)</TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="2.A.6.1.6"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=2.A.6.1.6&tcname=">2.A.6.1.6</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>The Zn<sup>2+</sup>-specific exporter, ZneABC. The ZneB MFP plays an active role in substrate efflux through metal binding and release. Its 2.8 &Aring; structure is available (<span id="ref8173.20534468" class="cite">De Angelis <em>et al.</em>, 2010</span>).&nbsp; 3.0 &Aring;&nbsp; intermediate conformational structures of ZneA have been determined, revealing two Zn<sup>2+</sup> binding sites separated by a channel, and the protein has been shown to catalyze electrogenic Zn<sup>2+</sup>:H<sup>+ </sup>antiport (<span id="ref10396.24173033" class="cite">Pak <em>et al.</em> 2013</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Proteobacteria</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>ZneABC of <em>Cupriavidus (Ralstonia) metallidurans (eutrophus or eutropha)</em> <br />ZneC (DMF) (Q1LCD9)<br />ZneA (RND) (Q1LCD8)<br />ZneB (MFP) (Q1LCD7)</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="2.A.6.1.7"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=2.A.6.1.7&tcname=">2.A.6.1.7</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Putative Zn<sup>2+</sup>&nbsp;exporter, Cus1ABC (induced by Zn<sup>2+</sup>; <span id="ref9011.20562277" class="cite">Moraleda-Mu&ntilde;oz <em>et al.</em>, 2010</span>)</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>&delta;-Proteobacteria</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Cus1ABC of <em>Myxococcus xanthus</em>&nbsp;<br />Cus1A (RND) (Q1DDM9)<br />Cus1B (MFP) (Q1DDM8)&nbsp;<br />Cus1C (OMF) (Q1DDM7)&nbsp;</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="2.A.6.1.8"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=2.A.6.1.8&tcname=">2.A.6.1.8</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Putative Cu<sup>2+</sup>&nbsp;exporter, Cus2ABC (induced by Cu<sup>2+</sup>; <span id="ref9011.20562277" class="cite">Moraleda-Mu&ntilde;oz <em>et al.</em>, 2010</span>)</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>&alpha;-Proteobacteria</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Czc1ABC of <em>Myxococcus xanthus</em><br />Czc1A (RND) (Q1D6S7)<br />Czc1B (MFP) (Q1D6S8)<br />Czc1C (OMF) (Q1D6S9)<br /><em>&nbsp;</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD COLSPAN=5><B>Examples:</B><P></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD>TC#</TD><TD>Name</TD><TD>Organismal Type</TD><TD>Example</TD></TR><TR><TD COLSPAN=5><BR><BR></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD COLSPAN=5><b><A HREF="tccluster.php?cluster=2.A.6.2">2.A.6.2 The (Largely Gram-negative Bacterial) Hydrophobe/Amphiphile Efflux-1 (HAE1) Family</A></b></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff"><TD COLSPAN=5><p><BR></TD></TR><TR><TD COLSPAN=5><B>Examples:</B><P></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD>TC#</TD><TD>Name</TD><TD>Organismal Type</TD><TD>Example</TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="2.A.6.2.1"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=2.A.6.2.1&tcname=">2.A.6.2.1</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Multidrug (acriflavin, doxorubicin, ethidium, rhodamine 6G, SDS, deoxycholate) resistance pump [required for normal chromosomal condensation and segregation as well as cell division] (<span id="ref6257.16267305" class="cite">Lau and Zgurskaya, 2005</span>). Exports L-cysteine (<span id="ref7184.16820466" class="cite">Yamada <em>et al.</em>, 2006</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Gram-negative bacteria</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>AcrEF (EnvCD) of <em>E. coli</em> <br /> AcrE (MFP) (P24180) <br /> AcrF (EnvD) (RND) (P24181)</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="2.A.6.2.10"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=2.A.6.2.10&tcname=">2.A.6.2.10</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC>Solvent efflux pump, TtgDEF (extrudes only toluene and styrene) (<span id="ref4609.17986203" class="cite">Teran <em>et al</em>., 2007</span>).</TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC>Gram-negative bacteria</TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC>TtgDEF of <I>Pseudomonas putida</I>: <br>

TtgGHI is the same as SrpABC (<span id="ref1223.9417051" class="cite">Kieboom <em>et al.</em>, 1998</span>)<br /</TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Gram-negative bacteria</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>TtgGHI of <em>Pseudomonas putida</em> <br /> TtgG (Q93PU5) <br /> TtgH (Q93PU4) <br /> TtgI (Q93PU3)</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="2.A.6.2.12"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=2.A.6.2.12&tcname=">2.A.6.2.12</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p><a></a></p>

<p><!-- test--></p>
<p><a id=""2.A.6.2.12""></a></p>
<p>Heteromeric multidrug/detergent resistance protein YegM/YegN/YegO (MdtA/MdtB/MdtC) (<span id=""ref1914.11566977"" class=""cite"">Nishino and Yamaguchi 2001</span>). Exports nalidixic acid, norfloxacin, cloxicillin, enoxacin, kanamycin, benzalkonium, bile salts, SDS and deoxycholate. It forms a complex with MdtA (YegM) (an MFP, TC# 8.A.1.6.2). Drug resistance depends on the simultaneous presence of all three proteins (<a class=""reflink"" href=""result.php?tc=2.A.6#ref2159"">Baranova and Nikaido, 2002</a>). (Also contributes to copper and zinc resistance; regulation is mediated by BaeSR, and indole, Cu<sup>2+ </sup> and Zn<sup>2+ </sup> induce (<a class=""reflink"" href=""result.php?tc=2.A.6#ref4414"">Nishino <em>et al</em>., 2007</a>)). MdtB:C stoichiometry = 2:1; MdtB and MdtC may play different roles (<a class=""reflink"" href=""result.php?tc=2.A.6#ref6842"">Kim <em>et al.</em>, 2010</a>), MdtB transporting the proton and MdtC transporting the drug (<a class=""reflink"" href=""result.php?tc=2.A.6#ref9231"">Kim and Nikaido 2012</a>).&nbsp; MdtBC is reported to export bile salts without MdtA (<span id=""ref2161.12107133"" class=""cite"">Nagakubo <em>et al.</em> 2002</span>), but this conclusion seems questionable.</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Bacteria</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>MdtB/MdtC of <em>E. coli</em><br />MdtB (YegN) (P76398)<br />MdtC (YegO) (P76399)&nbsp;</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="2.A.6.2.13"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=2.A.6.2.13&tcname=">2.A.6.2.13</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Multidrug/dye/detergent resistance protein, YhiU/YhiV or MdtE/MdtF (<span id="ref1914.11566977" class="cite">Nishino and Yamaguchi 2001</span>) MdtE (YhiU) is listed under TC# 8.A.1.6.3.&nbsp; The system exports erythromycin, doxorubicin, crystal violet, ethidium, rhodamine 6G, TPP, benzalkonium, SDS, deoxycholate and growth inhibitory steroid hormones (estradiol and progesterone) (<span id="ref3700.16428427" class="cite">Elkins and Mullis, 2006</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Bacteria</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>YhiUV or MdtEF of <em>E. coli</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="2.A.6.2.14"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=2.A.6.2.14&tcname=">2.A.6.2.14</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>SmeVWX MDR efflux pump. Drugs include chloramphenicol, quinolones, tetracyclines and aminoglycosides, but not &beta;-lactams and erythromycin (<span id="ref9219.21930878" class="cite">Chen <em>et al.</em>, 2011</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Proteobacteria</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>SmeVWX of <em>Stenotrophomonas maltophilia</em><br />SmeV (MFP) (B2FLY3)<br />SmeW (RND) (B2FLY4)<br />SmeX (OMF) (B2FLY6)<em>&nbsp;</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="2.A.6.2.15"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=2.A.6.2.15&tcname=">2.A.6.2.15</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Multidrug efflux pump, MexCD-OprJ (exports &beta;-lactams, fluoroquinolones, tetracycline, macrolides, chloramphenicol, biocides, including levofloxacin, carbenicillin, aztreonam, ceftazidime, cefepime, cefoperazone, piperacillin, erythromycin, azithromycin, chloramphenicol, etc.; <span id="ref2327.12410844" class="cite">Mao <em>et al</em>., 2002</span>).  Functions with MexC (MFP) and OprJ (OMF) as indicated above (<span id="ref2327.12410844" class="cite">Mao <em>et al</em>., 2002</span>; <span id="ref6067." class="cite">Poole, 2008</span>; <span id="ref34555764.36555423" class="cite">Lorusso <em>et al.</em> 2022</span>). MexCD-OprJ is one of four primary drug exporters in <em>P. aeruginosa</em> (<a class="reflink" href="../../search/result.php?tc=2.A.6#ref34555764">Lorusso <em>et al.</em> 2022</a>). Collateral sensitivity (CS) is an evolutionary trade-off traditionally 
linked to the mutational acquisition of antibiotic resistance (AR) (<span id="ref34556200.36997518" class="cite">Hernando-Amado <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>). 
However, AR can be temporally induced. Mutational 
acquisition of ciprofloxacin resistance leads to robust CS to tobramycin
 in pre-existing antibiotic-resistant mutants of <em>Pseudomonas aeruginosa</em>.
 Further, the strength of this phenotype is higher when <em>nfxB</em> mutants, 
over-producing the efflux pump MexCD-OprJ, are selected.&nbsp;<span id="ref34556200.36997518" class="cite">Hernando-Amado <em>et al.</em> 2023</span> induced
 transient <em>nfxB</em>-mediated ciprofloxacin resistance by using the 
antiseptic dequalinium chloride. Notably, non-inherited induction of AR 
renders transient tobramycin CS in the analyzed antibiotic-resistant 
mutants and clinical isolates, including tobramycin-resistant isolates. 
Further, by combining tobramycin with dequalinium chloride we drive 
these strains to extinction. Our results support that transient CS could
 allow the design of new evolutionary strategies to tackle 
antibiotic-resistant infections, avoiding the acquisition of AR 
mutations on which inherited CS depends (<span id="ref34556200.36997518" class="cite">Hernando-Amado <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>). The type of quinolone resistance mechanism is related to the frequency of
 MDRP and the risk of MDRP incidence is highly dependent on the 
order of exposure to gentamicin and ciprofloxacin (<span id="ref34556542.37258635" class="cite">Yasuda <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Bacteria</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>MexD of <em>Pseudomonas aeruginosa</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="2.A.6.2.16"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=2.A.6.2.16&tcname=">2.A.6.2.16</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Multidrug efflux pump, MexEF-OprN (exports fluoroquinolones, chloramphenicol, biocides, and xenobiotics; functions with MexE (MFP) and OprN (OMF)) as noted above (<span id="ref3188.9044268" class="cite">Kohler <em>et al</em>., 1997</span>; <span id="ref6067." class="cite">Poole, 2008</span>; <span id="ref34555764.36555423" class="cite">Lorusso <em>et al.</em> 2022</span>). The <em>P. putida</em> orthologue also exports solvents such as farnesyl hexanoate (<span id="ref34543722.21556065" class="cite">Dunlop <em>et al.</em> 2011</span>). MexAB-OprM is one of four primary drug exporters in <em>P. aeruginosa</em> (<a class="reflink" href="https://tcdb.org/search/result.php?tc=2.A.6#ref34555764">Lorusso <em>et al.</em> 2022</a>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Proteobacteria</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>MexF of <em>Pseudomonas aeruginosa</em> (AAG05882)</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="2.A.6.2.17"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=2.A.6.2.17&tcname=">2.A.6.2.17</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Multidrug efflux pump, MexK (exports fluoroquinolones, macrolides, chloramphenicol; biocides, and triclosan [with MexJ but without OprM] as well as tetracycline, erythromycin [requiring both MexJ and OprM]; <span id="ref2326.12193619" class="cite">Chuanchuen <em>et al</em>., 2002</span>).  Can function with OpmH (BAC24099) instead of OprM (<span id="ref6067." class="cite">Poole, 2008</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Bacteria</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>MexK of <em>Pseudomonas aeruginosa</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="2.A.6.2.18"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=2.A.6.2.18&tcname=">2.A.6.2.18</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>The polycyclic aromatic hydrocarbon (phenanthrene; anthacene; fluoranthene)/drug (chloramphenicol; nalidixic acid) exporter, EmhABC (<span id="ref2608.14563857" class="cite">Hearn <em>et al</em>., 2003</span>; 2006)</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Bacteria</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>EmhABC of <em>Pseudomonas fluorescens</em> <br /> EmhA (Q6V6X9) <br /> EmhB (Q6V6X8) <br /> EmhC (Q6V6X7)</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="2.A.6.2.19"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=2.A.6.2.19&tcname=">2.A.6.2.19</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC>The multidrug efflux pump, EefABC (exports chloramphenicol, ciprofloxacin, erythromycin, tetracycline and doxycycline) (<span id="ref3076.15901719" class="cite">Masi <em>et al</em>., 2005</span>). EefC exhibits low ionic selectivity (<span id="ref5509.17658457" class="cite">Masi <em>et al</em>., 2007</span>).</TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC>Bacteria</TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC>EefABC of <i>Enterobacter aerogenes</i> <br>
EefA (MFP) (Q8GC84) <br>
EefB (RND) (Q8GC83) <br>
EefC (OMF) (Q8GC82)  </TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="2.A.6.2.2"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=2.A.6.2.2&tcname=">2.A.6.2.2</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Multidrug/dye/detergent/bile salt/organic solvent resistance pump (substrates include: chloramphenicol, tetracycline, erythromycin, nalidixic acid, fusidic acid, fluoroquinolones, lipophilic &beta;-lactams, norfloxacin, doxorubicin, novobiocin, rifampin, trimethoprim, acriflavin, crystal violet, ethidium, disinfectants, rhodamine-6G, TPP, benzalkonium, SDS, Triton X-100, deoxycholate/bile salts/organic solvents (alkanes), growth inhibitory steroid hormones (estradiola and progesterone), and phospholipids) (<span>Elkins and Mullis, 2006</span>). Lateral entry of substrates from the lipid bilayer into AcrB and its homologues has been proposed (<span>Yu <em>et al.</em>, 2003a</span>; <span>2003b</span>). [An asymmetric trimeric structure is established with AcrA having a hexameric structure, and TolC having a trimeric structure (<span>Seeger <em>et al</em>., 2006</span>]. A structure of a complex with YajC is also known (T&ouml;rnroth-<span>Horsefield <em>et al</em>., 2007</span>). A covalently linked trimer of AcrB provides evidence for a peristaltic pump, alternative access, rotation mechanism (<span>Takatsuka and Nikaido, 2009</span>;<span>Nikaido and Takatsuka, 2009</span>; <span>Pos, 2009</span>) Further evidence for a rotatory mechanisms stems from kinetic analyses for cephalosporin efflux which can exhibit positive cooperativity (<span>Nagano and Nikaido, 2009</span>). May also export signaling molecules for cell-cell communication (<span>Yang <em>et al.</em>, 2006</span>). The substrates may be captured in the lower cleft region of AcrB, then transported through the binding pocket, the gate, and finally to the AcrA funnel that connects AcrB to TolC (<span>Husain &amp; Nikaido <em>et al.</em>, 2010</span>).&nbsp; AcrB has been converted into a light-driven proton pump using delta-rhodopsin (dR) linked to AcrB via a glycophorin A transmembrane domain. This created a
solar powered protein capable of selectively capturing antibiotics from bulk solutions (<span>Kapoor and Wendell 2013</span>).&nbsp; The trimeric structure is essential for activity (<span>Ye <em>et al.</em> 2014</span>).&nbsp; Association with AcrZ (TC# 8.A.50), a small 1 TMS protein (49 aas) that modifies the substrate specificity of AcrAB, has been demonstrated (<span>Hobbs <em>et al.</em> 2012</span>).&nbsp; In a similar way, the binding of YajC to AcrB stimulates the export of ampicillin (<span>T&ouml;rnroth-Horsefield <em>et al.</em> 2007</span>). AcrZ binds to
AcrB in a concave surface of the transmembrane domain (<span>Du <em>et al.</em> 2015</span>).&nbsp; Substrate binding accelerates conformational transitions and substrate dissociation, demonstrating cooperativity (<span>Wang <em>et al.</em> 2015</span>). The overall structure of AcrAB-TolC exemplifies
the adaptor bridging model, wherein the funnel-like AcrA hexamer forms an intermeshing cogwheel
interaction with the alpha-barrel tip region of TolC. Direct interaction between AcrB and TolC
is not allowed (<span>Kim <em>et al.</em> 2015</span>).&nbsp; TMS2 in AcrB is required for lipophilic carboxylate binding. A groove shaped by the interface between TMS1 and TMS2 specifically binds fusidic acid and other lipophilic
carboxylated drugs (<span>Oswald <em>et al.</em> 2016</span>). After ligand binding, a proton may bind to an acidic residue(s) in the transmembrane domain, i.e., Asp407 or Asp408, within the putative network of electrostatically interacting residues, which also include Lys940 and Thr978, and this may initiate a series of conformational changes that result in drug expulsion (<span>Su <em>et al.</em> 2006</span>). His978 is probably on the H<sup>+</sup> pathway (<span>Takatsuka and Nikaido 2006</span>). AcrAB-TolC segregates to the old pole following cell division, causing the two daughter cells to exhibit different drug resistances (<span>Bergmiller <em>et al.</em> 2017</span>). The hoisting-loop is a highly flexible hinge that enables conformational energy transmission (<span>Zwama <em>et al.</em> 2017</span>). AcrB exhibits three distinct conformational states in the transport cycle, substrate access, binding, and extrusion, or loose (L), tight (T), and open (O) states, respectively (<span>Yue <em>et al.</em> 2017</span>). Simulations show that both Asp407 and Asp408 are deprotonated in the L/T states, while only Asp408 is protonated in the O state. Release of a proton from Asp408 in the O state results in large conformational changes.&nbsp; Simulations offer dynamic details of how proton release drives the O-to-L transition in AcrB (<span>Yue <em>et al.</em> 2017</span>).&nbsp; The three-dimensional structures of the homo-trimer complexes of AcrB-like transporters, and a three-step functional rotation helps to explain the mechanism of transport, but a more comprehensive model has been proposed (<span>Zhang <em>et al.</em> 2017</span>). Preparation of the trimeric complex (AcrAB/TolC) for cryo EM has been described (<span>Du <em>et al.</em> 2018</span>). The structural and energetic basis behind coupling functional rotation to proton translocation has been presented (<span>Matsunaga <em>et al.</em> 2018</span>). Protonation of&nbsp; transmembrane Asp408 in the drug-bound protomer drives rotation. The conformational pathway identifies vertical shear motions among several transmembrane helices, which regulate alternate access of water as well as peristaltic motions that pump drugs into the periplasm (<span>Matsunaga <em>et al.</em> 2018</span>). CryoEM of detergent-free AcrB preserves lipid-protein interactions for visualization and reveals how the lipids pack against the protein (<span id="ref34549131.30509977" class="cite">Qiu <em>et al.</em> 2018</span>). In the presence of translation-inhibiting antibiotics, resistance 
acquisition depends on the AcrAB-TolC multidrug efflux pump, because it 
reduces tetracycline concentrations in the cell. Protein synthesis can 
thus persist and TetA expression can be initiated immediately after 
plasmid acquisition. AcrAB-TolC efflux activity can also preserve 
resistance acquisition by plasmid transfer in the presence of 
antibiotics with other modes of action (<span id="ref34549799.31123134" class="cite">Nolivos <em>et al.</em> 2019</span>). Multiple transport pathways within AcrB are tuned to substrate physicochemical properties related to the polyspecificity of the pump (<span id="ref34550641.31881208" class="cite">Tam <em>et al.</em> 2019</span>). The cryoEM structure of AcrB in an artificial membrane at 3.9 &Aring; resolution has been solved (<span id="ref34551918.32680969" class="cite">Yao <em>et al.</em> 2020</span>). The indole-dependent transport mechanism has been examined (<span id="ref34552499.31998988" class="cite">Jewel <em>et al.</em> 2020</span>). AcrB subunits rimerize to form the minimal functional unit, stabilized noncovalently by helix-helix interactions between TMS 1 and TMS 8. Peptides resembling these two TMSs inhibit subunit associations and drug export activity (<span id="ref34552952.33026802" class="cite">Jesin <em>et al.</em> 2020</span>). AcrB cycles between three functionally interdependent protomers through the loose (L), tight (T) and open (O) states during cooperative catalysis.&nbsp;<span id="ref34553675.34188038" class="cite">Tam <em>et al.</em> 2021</span> presented 13 X-ray structures of AcrB in intermediate states of the transport cycle. Structure-based mutational analysis combined with drug susceptibility assays indicated that drugs are guided through dedicated transport channels toward the drug binding pockets. A co-structure obtained in the combined presence of erythromycin, linezolid, oxacillin and fusidic acid showed binding of fusidic acid deeply inside the T protomer transmembrane domain. Thiol cross-link substrate protection assays indicated that this transmembrane domain-binding site can also accommodate oxacillin or novobiocin but not erythromycin or linezolid. AcrB-mediated drug transport is suggested to be allosterically modulated in the presence of multiple drugs (<span id="ref34553675.34188038" class="cite">Tam <em>et al.</em> 2021</span>). Phenylalanine-arginine &beta;-naphthylamide (PA&beta;N) is an inhibitor of efflux pumps, including AcrAB, in Gram-negative bacteria (<span id="ref34556527.37240007" class="cite">Al-Marzooq <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>). <em>Acacia senegal</em> budmunchiamines are adjuvants for rejuvenating phenicol activities towards <em>Escherichia coli</em>-AcrAB-mediated&nbsp; drug resistant strains (<span id="ref34556529.37240134" class="cite">Dofini Magnini <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>). Pyridylpiperazine-based inhibitors of AcrAB have been characterized (<span id="ref34554332.35013254" class="cite">Pl&eacute; <em>et al.</em> 2022</span>). Comparative reassessment of AcrB efflux inhibitors reveals differential impact of specific pump mutations on the activity of potent compounds (<span id="ref34557878.38170977" class="cite">Schuster <em>et al.</em> 2024</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Proteobacteria</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>AcrABZ of <em>E. coli</em> <br /> AcrA (MFP) (P31223) <br /> AcrB (RND) (P31224)<br />AcrZ of 49 aas (P0AAW9)</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="2.A.6.2.20"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=2.A.6.2.20&tcname=">2.A.6.2.20</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC>The toxoflavin (a phytotoxin) exporter, ToxGHI (<span id="ref3116.15522077" class="cite">Kim <em>et al</em>., 2004</span>)</TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC>Bacteria</TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC>ToxGHI of <i>Burkholderia glumae</i>  <br>

as organic solvents by their periplasmic domains and expel them to the 
external milieu (<span>Li <em>et al.</em> 2006</span>). These structures allowed them to directly observe various novel conformational states of the AdeB trimer. The transmembrane region of trimeric AdeB can associate with formation of a trimeric assembly or dissociated into "dimer plus monomer" and "monomer plus monomer plus monomer" configurations. A single AdeB protomer can simultaneously anchor a number of ethidium ligands, and different AdeB protomers can bind ethidium molecules simultaneously. A drug transport mechanism was proposed that involves multiple multidrug-binding sites and various transient states of the AdeB membrane protein. with the AcrAB efflux pump (<span id="ref34550736.30468763" class="cite">Iyer <em>et al.</em> 2019</span>). Opening of the outer membrane protein channel, MtrE, in the tripartite efflux pump, MtrCDE, is induced by interaction with the membrane fusion partner, MtrC (<span id="ref7447.21115481" class="cite">Janganan <em>et al.</em>, 2011</span>).&nbsp; The crystal structure of the trimeric MtrE forms a
vertical tunnel extending down contiguously from the outer membrane surface to the periplasmic end in the open conformational state of this channel (<span id="ref11273.24901251" class="cite">Lei <em>et al.</em> 2014</span>). Coordination of substrate binding and protonation in MtrD controls the functionally rotating transport mechanism (<span id="ref34553280.33980014" class="cite">Fairweather <em>et al.</em> 2021</span>). The amino acyl sequence, N917-P927, plays a key role in modulating substrate access to the binding cleft and influences the overall orientation of the protein within the inner membrane necessary for optimal functioning (<span id="ref34553785.34465021" class="cite">Chitsaz <em>et al.</em> 2021</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Gram-negative proteobacteria</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>MtrCDE of <em>Neisseria gonorrhoeae</em>: <br /> MtrC (MFP) (P43505) <br /> MtrD (RND) (Q51073) <br /> MtrE (OMF) (Q51006)</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="2.A.6.2.50"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=2.A.6.2.50&tcname=">2.A.6.2.50</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>AcrB multidrug exporter of 1,032 aas and 12 TMSs (<span id="ref34550694.31531401" class="cite">Zwama <em>et al.</em> 2019</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>AcrB of <em>Haemophilus influenzae</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="2.A.6.2.6"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=2.A.6.2.6&tcname=">2.A.6.2.6</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p><a></a></p>
<p><!-- test--></p>
<p><a></a></p>
<p>Multiple drug; N-(3-oxododecanoyl)-L-homoserine lactone autoinducer efflux pump, MexB (functions with MexA (an MFP, 8.A.1) and OprM (an OMF, 1.B.17; see 2.A.6.2.21). All three interact with each other. MexA promotes assembly and stability of the complex (<a>Nehme and Poole, 2007</a>)). Exports &beta;-lactams, fluoroquinolones, tetracycline, macrolides, chloramphenicol, biocides, and a toxic indole compound, CBR-4830, that targets the MreB actin (<a>Robertson <em>et al</em>., 2007</a>). Confers tolerance to tea tree oil and its monoterpene components Terpinen-4-ol, 1,8-cineole and &alpha;-terpineol (<a>Papadopoulos <em>et al.</em>, 2008</a>) as well as the antimicrobial peptide, colistin (<a>Pamp <em>et al</em>., 2008</a>) (<a>Mao <em>et al</em>., 2002</a>; <a>Poole, 2008</a>). The crystal structure has been solved at 3.0 &Aring; resolution (<a>Sennhauser <em>et al.</em>, 2009</a>). The MexA-OprM complex has an elongated cylindrical appearance (<a>Tr&eacute;pout <em>et al.</em>, 2010</a>).&nbsp; Mutations affecting export of antibiotics with cytoplasmic targets have been identified (<a>Ohene-Agyei <em>et al.</em> 2012</a>). RND-type xenobiotic transporters recognize hydrophobic substrates such as organic solvents by their periplasmic domains and expel them to the external milieu (<span>Li <em>et al.</em> 2006</span>). Differential impacts of MexB mutations on substrate selectivity of the MexAB-OprM multidrug efflux pump have been reported (<span id="ref34553778.14973037" class="cite">Middlemiss and Poole 2004</span>). MexAB-OprM is one of four primary drug exporters in <em>P. aeruginosa</em> (<span id="ref34555764.36555423" class="cite">Lorusso <em>et al.</em> 2022</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Gram-negative bacteria</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>MexAB of <em>Pseudomonas aeruginosa<br /></em>MexA (P52477)<br />MexB (P52002)</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="2.A.6.2.7"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=2.A.6.2.7&tcname=">2.A.6.2.7</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Multidrug efflux pump, AcrD (exports aminoglycosides (amikacin, gentamycin, neomycin, kanamycin and tobramycin) as well as anionic detergents (SDS and deoxycholate) and growth inhibitory steroid hormones (estradiol and progesterone)(<span id="ref3700.16428427" class="cite">Elkins and Mullis, 2006</span>)) (exports aminoglycosides from the periplasm as well as the cytoplasm) (<span id="ref3014.15743938" class="cite">Aires and Nikaido, 2005</span>). (Also contributes to copper and zinc resistance; regulation is mediated by BaeSR, and indole, Cu<sup>2+</sup> and Zn<sup>2+</sup> induce (<span id="ref4414.17933888" class="cite">Nishino <em>et al</em>., 2007</span>)). Exports L-cysteine (<span id="ref7184.16820466" class="cite">Yamada <em>et al.</em>, 2006</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Gram-negative bacteria</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>AcrD of <em>E. coli</em> (P24177)</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="2.A.6.2.8"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=2.A.6.2.8&tcname=">2.A.6.2.8</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Multidrug efflux pump, ArpB (exports tetracycline, chloramphenicol, carbenicillin, streptomycin, erythromycin, novobiocin, etc.)</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Gram-negative bacteria</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>ArpB of<em> Pseudomonas putida</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="2.A.6.2.9"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=2.A.6.2.9&tcname=">2.A.6.2.9</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Solvent efflux pump, TtgABC (extrudes toluene, styrene, m-xylene, ethylbenzene, acetate,&nbsp;&alpha;-pinene and propylbenzene) (<span id="ref4609.17986203" class="cite">Teran <em>et al</em>., 2007; HREF="tccluster.php?cluster=2.A.6.4">2.A.6.4 The SecDF (SecDF) Family</A></b></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff"><TD COLSPAN=5><p><BR></TD></TR><TR><TD COLSPAN=5><B>Examples:</B><P></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD>TC#</TD><TD>Name</TD><TD>Organismal Type</TD><TD>Example</TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="2.A.6.4.1"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=2.A.6.4.1&tcname=">2.A.6.4.1</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>The secretory accessory proteins, SecDF. The first periplasmic domain of SecDF has been crystallized (<span id="ref8195.22102233" class="cite">Echizen <em>et al.</em>, 2011</span>) as has the intact SecDF complex (<span id="ref34546128.27857601" class="cite">Tsukazaki and Nureki 2011</span>). SecDF has been reported to function as a pmf-driven H<sup>+</sup>&nbsp;transporter that facilitates protein translocation (<span id="ref10120.21562494" class="cite">Tsukazaki <em>et al.</em> 2011</span>).&nbsp; It may assume at least two conformations
differing by a 120 degrees rotation during polypeptide translocation (<span id="ref34543988.24368747" class="cite">Mio <em>et al.</em> 2014</span>). SecDF is proposed to undergo repeated conformational transitions to pull
 out the precursor protein from the SecYEG channel into the periplasm (<span id="ref34549179.29718185" class="cite">Tsukazaki 2018</span>). 
Once SecDF captures the precursor protein on the periplasmic surface, it can complete protein translocation even if SecA function is 
inactivated by ATP depletion, implying that SecDF is a 
protein-translocation motor that works independent of SecA. Structural 
and functional analyses of SecDF suggested that SecDF utilizes 
the proton gradient and interacts with precursor proteins in the flexible
 periplasmic region. The crystal structures of SecDF in different states
 at more than 3 &Aring; resolution were reported in 2017 and 2018, which 
further improved our understanding of the dynamic molecular mechanisms 
of SecDF (<span id="ref34549179.29718185" class="cite">Tsukazaki 2018</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Proteobacteria</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>SecDF of <em>E. coli</em>; SecD; SecF</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="2.A.6.4.2"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=2.A.6.4.2&tcname=">2.A.6.4.2</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC>Protein translocase subunit SecDF</TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Bacteria</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>SecDF of <em>Bacillus subtilis </em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="2.A.6.4.3"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=2.A.6.4.3&tcname=">2.A.6.4.3</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Protein translocase subunit SecDF.&nbsp; The 3-dimensional structure is known at 3.3 &Aring; resolution (<span id="ref10120.21562494" class="cite">Tsukazaki <em>et al.</em> 2011</span>).&nbsp; SecDF serves several functions, such as stabilizing other Sec translocon components within the membrane, maintaining the transmembrane (TM) potential, and facilitating the ATP-independent stage of the translocation mechanism. SecDF also undergoes functionally important conformational changes that involve mainly its P1-head domain, and these changes are coupled with the proton motive force (&Delta;pmf). Using all-atom molecular dynamics simulations combined with umbrella sampling, <span id="ref34547274.28636909" class="cite">Ficici <em>et al.</em> 2017</span> studied the P1-head conformational change and how it is coupled to the pmf. They reported potentials of mean force along a root-mean-square-distance-based reaction coordinate obtained in the presence and absence of the TM electrical potential. Their results showed that the interaction of the P1 domain dipole moment with the TM electrical field lowers the free-energy barrier in the direction of the F-form to I-form transition, <span>two conformations that vary by the relative positioning of the P1-head 
subdomain&mdash;the large periplasmic domain of TtSecDF&mdash;which is suggested to 
undergo a hinge motion (</span><span><span id="ref34547274.28636909" class="cite">Ficici <em>et al.</em> 2017</span>).<br /></span></p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Bacteria</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>SecDF of <em>Thermus thermophilus </em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="2.A.6.4.4"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=2.A.6.4.4&tcname=">2.A.6.4.4</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>SecDF of 1254 aas and 12 TMSs</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>SecDF of <em>Spiroplasma diminutum</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD COLSPAN=5><BR><BR></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD COLSPAN=5><b><A HREF="tccluster.php?cluster=2.A.6.5">2.A.6.5 The (Gram-positive Bacterial Putative) Hydrophobe/Amphiphile Efflux-2 (HAE2) Family</A></b></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff"><TD COLSPAN=5><p><BR></TD></TR><TR><TD COLSPAN=5><B>Examples:</B><P></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD>TC#</TD><TD>Name</TD><TD>Organismal Type</TD><TD>Example</TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="2.A.6.5.1"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=2.A.6.5.1&tcname=">2.A.6.5.1</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC>The antibiotic actinorhodin transport-associated protein, ActII3 </TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC>Gram-positive bacteria </TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC>ActII3 of <I>Streptomyces coelicolor</I> </TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="2.A.6.5.10"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=2.A.6.5.10&tcname=">2.A.6.5.10</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Multidrug resistance protein, CmpL1, of 772 aas and 12 TMSs. in minimal medium and accumulate intracellular trehalose 
monocorynomycolates, free corynomycolates, and a previously 
uncharacterized corynomycolate-containing lipid.&nbsp; It is inferred that this transporter exports one or more of these lipids.&nbsp; Evidence for a pmf-dependent mechanism was obtained (<span id="ref34546023.24942069" class="cite">Yang <em>et al.</em> 2014</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>CmpL1 of <em>Corynebacterium glutamicum </em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="2.A.6.5.11"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=2.A.6.5.11&tcname=">2.A.6.5.11</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>CmpL4 of 801 aas and 12 TMSs.&nbsp; Multidrug resistance protein of 801 aas and 12 TMSs. this transporter exports one or more of these lipids.&nbsp; Evidence for a 
pmf-dependent mechanism was obtained (<a class="reflink" href="http://www.tcdb.org/search/result.php?tc=2.A.6#ref34546023">Yang <em>et al.</em> 2014</a>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>CmpL4 of <em>Corynebacterium glutamicum</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="2.A.6.5.12"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=2.A.6.5.12&tcname=">2.A.6.5.12</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>MmpL4a of 959 aas and 12 TMSs.&nbsp; A rough morphotype has a Y842H mutation that causes a deficiency in

in vitro. In zebrafish, increased virulence of the<em> M. bolletii </em>R variant over the parental S strain
