death. Tyr842 is conserved in several MmpL proteins (<span id="ref34546327.26585558" class="cite">Bernut <em>et al.</em> 2016</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>MmpL4a of <em>Mycobacterium abscessus </em>subsp. bolletii</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="2.A.6.5.13"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=2.A.6.5.13&tcname=">2.A.6.5.13</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Lipid (acyl and diacyl trehalose) exporter of 1002 aas and 12 TMSs, MmpL10 (<span id=""ref34546328.25986884"" class=""cite"">Bailo <em>et al.</em> 2015</span>). A 12 TMS topology has been determined (<span id=""ref34548085.28610783"" class=""cite"">Belardinelli and Jackson 2017</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>MmpL10 of <em>Mycobacterium tuberculosis</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="2.A.6.5.14"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=2.A.6.5.14&tcname=">2.A.6.5.14</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Uncharacterized MmpL-like efflux pump of 843 aas and 12 TMSs</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>MmpL of <em>Entamoeba histolytica</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="2.A.6.5.15"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=2.A.6.5.15&tcname=">2.A.6.5.15</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Fatty acid efflux MMPL transporter, FarE, of 829 aas and 12 TMSs in a 1 + 3 + 2 + 1 + 3 + 2 TMS arrangement (<span id="ref34554042.34739144" class="cite">Malwal and Oldfield 2021</span>). FarE has the same set of "active-site" residues as those found in the mycobacterial MmpL3s (TC# 2.A.6.5.6), and in the homolog from <em>Trypanosoma cruzi</em> (TC# 2.A.6.5.16).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>FarE of <em>Staphylococcus aureus</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="2.A.6.5.16"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=2.A.6.5.16&tcname=">2.A.6.5.16</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>MmpL efflux pump of 866 aas and 12 TMSs (<span id="ref34554043.30355302" class="cite">Bradwell <em>et al.</em> 2018</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>MmpL pump of <em>Trypanosoma cruzi</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="2.A.6.5.2"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=2.A.6.5.2&tcname=">2.A.6.5.2</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>The lipid exporter, mycobacterial membrane protein Large-7, MmpL7, is required for the export of the virulence lipid, phthiocerol dimycocerosate (PDIM), in <em>Mycobacterium tuberculosis</em>. MmpL7. It also confers high level isoniazid efflux and resistance (<span id="ref7928.16251328" class="cite">Pasca <em>et al.</em>, 2005</span>). MmpL7 is also involved in the transport of the structurally related phenolic glycolipid (PGL).&nbsp;<span id="ref34553783.34522829" class="cite">Moolla <em>et al.</em> 2021</span> generated an in silico model of MmpL7 that revealed MmpL7 as a functional outlier within the MmpL family, missing a canonical proton-relay signature sequence, suggesting that it employs a yet-unidentified mechanism for energy coupling for transport. The periplasmic porter domain 2 insert (PD2-insert), which doesn't share recognisable homology, is highly alpha-helical in nature, suggesting an organisation similar to that seen in the hopanoid PD3/4 domains. They also identified residues present in the transmembrane domains TM4 and TM10, and the PD2 domain insert that play crucial roles in PDIM transport (<span id="ref34553783.34522829" class="cite">Moolla <em>et al.</em> 2021</span>).</p>
<p>&nbsp;</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Gram-positive bacteria</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>MmpL7 of <em>Mycobacterium tuberculosis</em> ( 	P65370)</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="2.A.6.5.3"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=2.A.6.5.3&tcname=">2.A.6.5.3</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>The putative glycopeptidolipid exporter, TmtpC (most similar to MmpL of <em>M. leprae</em>; implicated in sliding motility). May function with the MmpS4 protein of <em>Mucobacterium smegmatis </em>(A0QPN7) to form a scaffold for coupled biosynthesis and transport (<span id="ref7502.21062372" class="cite">Deshayes <em>et al.</em>, 2010</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Gram-positive bacteria</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>TmtpC of <em>Mycobacterium smegmatis</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="2.A.6.5.4"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=2.A.6.5.4&tcname=">2.A.6.5.4</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Sulfolipid, 2,3-diacyl-&alpha;, &alpha;'-D-trehalose-2'-sulfate (sulfatide precursor) exporter, MmpL8 (<span id="ref2823.15001577" class="cite">Domenech <em>et al</em>., 2004; <span id="ref9240.22194604" class="cite">Seeliger <em>et al.</em> 2012</span></span>). Mutations associated with tuberculosis in <em>mmpl8</em> as well as <em>mmpl3</em> and <em>mmpl9</em> have been isolated (<span id="ref34555073.35868449" class="cite">Khan <em>et al.</em> 2022</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Gram-positive bacteria</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>MmpL8 of <em>Mycobacterium tuberculosis</em> (CAB10022)</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="2.A.6.5.5"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=2.A.6.5.5&tcname=">2.A.6.5.5</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p><a></a></p>
<p><!-- test--></p>
<p><a></a></p>
<p>Mycobacterial heme acquisition system, Rv0202c - Rv0207c. Takes up free heme and heme from hemoglobin as an iron source together with the secreted protein, Rv0203 (O53654) (<span>Owens <em>et al.</em> 2013</span>). May function with Rv0206c (MmpL3; TC#2.A.6.5.6) and Rv0202c (<span>Tullius <em>et al.</em>, 2011</span>). However, see description of MmpL3 (2.A.6.5.6).&nbsp; These two proteins are targets of drug action (<span>Owens <em>et al.</em> 2013</span>). A 12 TMS topology has been suggested (<span>Belardinelli and Jackson 2017</span>). Mycobacterial membrane protein Large 3 (MmpL3) is a promising drug target because its activity is essential and required for cell-wall biosynthesis. Several classes of MmpL3 inhibitors have been developed against <em>Mycobacterium tuberculosis</em> (Mtb) with potent anti-TB activity, one of which is the drug candidate SQ109. <span id="ref34551665.32512002" class="cite">Yang <em>et al.</em> 2020</span> have determined crystal structures of MmpL3 in complex with 
NITD-349 and SPIRO. Both inhibitors bind deep in the central channel of 
the transmembrane (TM) domain and cause conformational changes to the 
protein. The amide nitrogen and indole nitrogen of NITD-349 and the 
piperidine nitrogen of SPIRO interact and clamp Asp645. Analysis of the two structures reveals that these inhibitors target the 
proton relay pathway to block the activity of MmpL3 (<span id="ref34551665.32512002" class="cite">Yang <em>et al.</em> 2020</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Actinobacteria</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Heme uptake system of <em>Mycobacterium tuberculosis</em><br />MmpL11 (P65374)<br /><br />&nbsp;</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="2.A.6.5.6"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=2.A.6.5.6&tcname=">2.A.6.5.6</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>The mycobacterial membrane protein large 3 (MmpL3) (Rv0206; 944 aas) may function with MmpL11 (TC# 2.A.6.5.5) (<span id="ref7816.21383189" class="cite">Tullius <em>et al.</em>, 2011</span>). MmpL3 exports trehalose monomycolate (TMM), involved in mycolic acid donation to the cell wall core (<span id="ref8251.22252828" class="cite">Tahlan <em>et al.</em>, 2012</span>). SQ109, a 1,2,-diamine related to ethambutol &nbsp;is an inhibitor of MmpL3 (<span id="ref8251.22252828" class="cite">Tahlan <em>et al.</em>, 2012</span>).&nbsp; The system may also transport heme.&nbsp; Inhibitors have been identified (<span id="ref10466.24325728" class="cite">Rayasam 2013</span>; <span id="ref11312.25136022" class="cite">Li <em>et al.</em> 2014</span>).&nbsp; MmpL3 has been shown to be a homotrimer of three 12 TMS subunits, confirming its RND-type structure (<span id="ref34546022.27737557" class="cite">Belardinelli <em>et al.</em> 2016</span>).&nbsp; MmpL3 is a flipppase for mycolic acids, transporting them from the cytoplasmic side of the inner membrane to the external side. A 1.5-diarylpyrrole compound, BM212, is a potent inhibitor (<span id="ref34549118.28698380" class="cite">Xu <em>et al.</em> 2017</span>). Inactivation of the <em>mmpL3</em> gene in <em>M. neoaurum</em> increased the permeability of the outer membrane and allowed increased uptake of sterols for coversion to other sterols for industrial purposes. One such product is 22-hydroxy-23,24-bisnorchol-4-ene-3-one (4-HBC), used for the synthesis of various steroids in the industry (<span id="ref34546806.28532497" class="cite">Xiong <em>et al.</em> 2017</span>). Since iron deprivation decreases expression of the <em>mmpL3</em> gene, a metal chelation strategy could boost the effectiveness of current anti-TB drug regimes to combat drug resistant TB (<span id="ref34549119.30430296" class="cite">Pal <em>et al.</em> 2018</span>). Crystal structures are available for MmpL3 alone and in complex with four TB drug candidates. MmpL3 consists of a periplasmic pore domain and a twelve-helix transmembrane domain. Two Asp-Tyr pairs centrally located in this domain appear to facilitate proton-translocation. SQ109, AU1235, ICA38, and rimonabant bind inside the transmembrane region and disrupt these Asp-Tyr pairs (<span id="ref34549428.30682372" class="cite">Zhang <em>et al.</em> 2019</span>). MmpL3 can be directly inhibited by several antitubercular compounds (<span id="ref34549661.30882198" class="cite">Li <em>et al.</em> 2019</span>). <span id="ref34551665.32512002" class="cite">Yang <em>et al.</em> 2020</span> have determined crystal structures of MmpL3 in complex with 
NITD-349 and SPIRO. Both inhibitors bind deep in the central channel of 
the transmembrane (TM) domain and cause conformational changes to the 
protein. The amide nitrogen and indole nitrogen of NITD-349 and the 
piperidine nitrogen of SPIRO interact and clamp Asp645. Analysis of the two structures reveals that these inhibitors target the 
proton relay pathway to block the activity of MmpL3 (<span id="ref34551665.32512002" class="cite">Yang <em>et al.</em> 2020</span>). The&nbsp; (MmpL3) transporter is required for shuttling the lipid trehalose monomycolate (TMM), a precursor of mycolic acid (MA)-containing trehalose dimycolate (TDM) and mycolyl arabinogalactan peptidoglycan (mAGP), in <em>Mycobacterium</em> species, including <em>Mycobacterium tuberculosis</em> and <em>Mycobacterium smegmatis</em>. It&nbsp; facilitates the transport of fatty acids and lipidic elements to the mycobacterial cell wall.&nbsp;<span id="ref34553521.34383749" class="cite">Su <em>et al.</em> 2021</span> reported 7 structures of the <em>M. smegmatis</em> MmpL3 transporter in its unbound state and in complex with trehalose 6-decanoate (T6D) or TMM using single-particle cryo-EM and X-ray crystallography. Combined with calculated results from molecular dynamics (MD) and target MD simulations, they revealed a lipid transport mechanism that involves a coupled movement of the periplasmic domain and transmembrane helices of the MmpL3 transporter that facilitates the shuttling of lipids to the mycobacterial cell wall (<span id="ref34553521.34383749" class="cite">Su <em>et al.</em> 2021</span>). Antibacterial compounds that target MmpL3 called ST004, have been identified and studied, showing that this compound strongly inhibits growth, and the cryoEM structure of MmpL3 with ST004 bound has been determined (<span id="ref34555130.35981536" class="cite">Hu <em>et al.</em> 2022</span>). PgfA (MSMEG_0317; TC# 1.B.163.1.9), a periplasmic protein that interacts with MmpL3, is a key determinant of polar growth and cell envelope composition 
in mycobacteria, and the LamA-mediated recruitment of this protein 
to one side of the cell is a required step in the establishment of 
cellular asymmetry (<span id="ref34555579.36346214" class="cite">Gupta <em>et al.</em> 2022</span>). Mycobacterial membrane protein large 3 (MmpL3) is an essential mycolic 
acid and lipid transporter required for growth and cell viability, and its function and inhibition have been reviewed (<span id="ref34555961.36809064" class="cite">Williams and Abramovitch 2023</span>). A continuous water pathway through the transmembrane region has been proposed, illustrating a putative pathway for protons. TMM can diffuse from 
the membrane into a binding pocket in MmpL3 spontaneously. Acetylation of TMM, which is required for transport, makes it more
 stable within MmpL3's periplasmic cavity compared to the unacetylated 
form (<span id="ref34556080.36926696" class="cite">Li <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>).&nbsp; Equilibrium simulations revealed that trehalose monosmycolate can diffuse from the membrane into a binding pocket in MmpL3 spontaneously. Acetylation of TMM, which is required for transport, makes it more stable within MmpL3's periplasmic cavity (<span id="ref34556080.36926696" class="cite">Li <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>). Consistent with the close-open motion of the two PD domains, TMM entry 
size changes in the apo system, likely loading and moving the TMM, but 
it does not vary much in the holo system and probably impairs the movement 
of the TMM (<span id="ref34557039.37589273" class="cite">Carbone <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>). Water molecules passed through
 the central channel of the MmpL3 transporter to the cytoplasmic side in
 the apo system but not in the holo system, with a mean passing time of 
&sim;135 ns. Because water wires play an essential role in transporting 
protons, these findings shed light on the importance of the PMF in driving the
 close-open motion of the two TMSs. The key channel
 residues involved in water passage display considerable overlap with 
conserved residues within the MmpL protein family, supporting their 
critical functional role (<span id="ref34557039.37589273" class="cite">Carbone <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>). The&nbsp; inhibitory mechanism of anti-TB drug SQ109 involves the allosteric inhibition of TMM translocation by MmpL3 (<span id="ref34557039.37589273" class="cite">Carbone <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Bacteria</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>MmpL3 of <em>Mycobacterium tuberculosis</em> (O53657)</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="2.A.6.5.7"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=2.A.6.5.7&tcname=">2.A.6.5.7</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p><a></a></p>
<p><!-- test--></p>
<p><a id="2.A.6.5.7"></a></p>
<p>Siderophore export transporter, MmpL4 (<a class="reflink" href="result.php?tc=2.A.6#ref10055">Wells <em>et al.</em> 2013</a>).&nbsp; Functions with MmpS4 (TC#8.A.35.1.1) which is essential for transport activity.&nbsp; MmpL4/MmpS4 and MmpL5/MmpS5 (TC# 2.A.6.5.8 and TC# 8.A.35.1.2, respectively) are two siderophore exporters that overlap in function (<a class="reflink" href="result.php?tc=2.A.6#ref10055">Wells <em>et al.</em> 2013</a>).&nbsp; The <em>M. abscessus</em>, subspecies bolletii orthologue (TC# 2.A.6.5.12), of 959 aas, is 65% identical to <em>M. tuberculosis</em> MmpL4 and affects the rough vs smooth phenotype of the cell envelope (<span id="ref34546327.26585558" class="cite">Bernut <em>et al.</em> 2016</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Actinobacteria</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>MmpL4 of <em>Mycobacterium tuberculosis</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="2.A.6.5.8"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=2.A.6.5.8&tcname=">2.A.6.5.8</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Siderophore exporter, MmpL5.&nbsp; Functions with MmpS5, and both proteins are essential for transport activitiy (<span id="ref10055.23431276" class="cite">Wells <em>et al.</em> 2013</span>). Together with MmpS5, it also pumps drugs out of the cell, and upregulation gives rise to drug resistance (<span id="ref34547375.29050760" class="cite">Briffotaux <em>et al.</em> 2017</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Actinobacteria</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>MmpL5 of <em>Mycobacterium tuberculosis</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="2.A.6.5.9"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=2.A.6.5.9&tcname=">2.A.6.5.9</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC>The MmpL-like protein of 1138 aas (sequence similarity is observed only in the hydrophilic extracytoplasmic regions of both proteins (residues 452-665 in PIP)<br /</TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Bacteria</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>MmpL-like protein of <em>Bacillus weihenstephanensis</em> (A9VJD5)</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR><TD COLSPAN=5><BR><BR></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD COLSPAN=5><b><A HREF="tccluster.php?cluster=2.A.6.6">2.A.6.6 The Eukaryotic (Putative) Sterol Transporter (EST) Family</A></b></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff"><TD COLSPAN=5><p><p>This family has the NPC1 fold (<span id='ref34555373.36164651' class='cite'>Ferrada and Superti-Furga 2022</span>).</p><BR></TD></TR><TR><TD COLSPAN=5><B>Examples:</B><P></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD>TC#</TD><TD>Name</TD><TD>Organismal Type</TD><TD>Example</TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="2.A.6.6.1"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=2.A.6.6.1&tcname=">2.A.6.6.1</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Niemann-Pick C1 (SLC65A1) and C2 disease proteins, NPC1 and NPC2, together may form a lipid/cholesterol exporter from lysosomes to other cellular sites including the plasma membrane (<a>Sleat <em>et al</em>., 2004; </a><span>Kennedy <em>et al.</em> 2014</span>). NPC1 or NPC2 deficiency causes lysosomal retention of cholesterol, sphingolipids, phospholipids, and glycolipids as well as neuronal dysfunction and neurodegeneration (<a>Infante <em>et al.</em> 2008a</a>). Cholesterol binds to the sterol-sensing domain (<span id="ref34552493.15314240" class="cite">Ohgami <em>et al.</em> 2004</span>). Increased mitochondrial cholesterol, observed in NPC1 or NPC2 deficiency, causes oxidative stress and increased rates of glycolysis and lactate release (<span>Kennedy <em>et al.</em> 2014</span>).&nbsp; NPC1 binds cholesterol, 25-hydroxycholesterol and various oxysterols (<a>Infante <em>et al.</em> 2008b</a>; 
<a>Liu <em>et al.</em>, 2009</a> ). Soluble NPC2 binds cholesterol, and then passes it to the N-terminal domain of membranous NPC1 (<a>Abi-Mosleh <em>et al.</em>, 2009</a>). Cholesterol trafficking in Niemann-Pick C-deficient cells was reviewed by <a>Peake and Vance (2010)</a>. NPC1 is a late-endosomal membrane protein involved in trafficking of LDL-
derived cholesterol, Niemann-Pick disease type C, and Ebola virus infection.&nbsp; It is the Ebola virus receptor. It contains 13 TMSs, five of which are thought to represent a "sterol-sensing domain", also present in other key regulatory proteins of cholesterol biosynthesis, uptake,
and signaling. A
crystal structure of a large fragment of human NPC1 at 3.6&nbsp;&Aring; resolution revealed internal
twofold pseudosymmetry along TMSs 2-13 and two structurally homologous domains that protrude 60&nbsp;&Aring; into
the endosomal lumen (<span>Li <em>et al.</em> 2016</span>). NPC1's sterol sensing domain forms a cavity that is accessible from both the luminal
bilayer leaflet and the endosomal lumen; this cavity is large
enough to accommodate one cholesterol molecule. A model was proposed for&nbsp; cholesterol
sensing and transport (<span>Li <em>et al.</em> 2016</span>).&nbsp; Lysosomal cholesterol activates TORC1 via an SLC38A9-Niemann-Pick C1 signaling complex (<span>Castellano <em>et al.</em> 2017</span>).&nbsp; <span>Gong <em>et al.</em> 2016 </span>presented a 4.4&nbsp;&Aring; structure of the full-length human NPC1 and a low-resolution
 reconstruction of NPC1 in complex with the cleaved glycoprotein (GPcl) 
of EBOV, both determined by single-particle electron cryomicroscopy. 
NPC1 contains three distinct lumenal
 domains A (also designated NTD), C, and I. TMSs 2-13 exhibit a typical RND fold, among which TMSs 3-7 constitute
 the sterol-sensing domain conserved in several proteins involved in 
cholesterol&nbsp;metabolism and signaling. A trimeric EBOV-GPcl binds to one 
NPC1 monomer through domain&nbsp;C (<span>Gong <em>et al.</em> 2016</span>). The effects of disease-causing mutations on quality control pathways involving the lysosome and endoplasmic reticulum, and how it functions to clear the most common mutant protein found in Niemann-Pick type C patients have been reviewed (<span>Schultz <em>et al.</em> 2016</span>). In the same review, knowledge concerning the mechanisms that degrade misfolded transmembrane proteins in the endoplasmic reticulum is presented. Cholesterol esters are components of low density lipoprotein (LDL), 
which is brought into the cells of various tissues by targeted 
endocytosis. Within the endosomes, cholesterol esters are hydrolyzed, 
releasing free cholesterol, which is finally exported out of the 
endosome by NPC1 with assistance from a soluble protein NPC2 (<span>Nikaido 2018</span>). The transmembrane helices in the N-terminal half (the SSD, 
sterol-sensing domain) of NPC1 are homologous to the
sterol-binding domains of HMG-CoA reductase, as well as the regulator of
 cholesterol-regulated transcription activation, SCAP. The domain that binds cholesterol with the highest affinity, within NPC1, however, is the NTD. In the NPC2-NPC1 complex, the substrate is captured at a location far 
away from the membrane by NPC2, and then is brought to a location close 
to the membrane surface (NTD of NPC1), and is finally moved to the 
intramembranous region of NPC1. Degradation occurs via two pathways, the proteasome following MARCH6-dependent ERAD, and an autophagic pathway called the selective ER autophagy (ER-phagy) (<span id="ref34549249.30202070" class="cite">Schultz <em>et al.</em> 2018</span>). NPC1 exports LDL-derived cholesterol from lysosomes by carrying it through the 80&nbsp;&Aring; glycocalyx and the 40&nbsp;&Aring; lipid bilayer. Transport begins when cholesterol binds to the N-terminal domain (NTD) of NPC1, which projects to the surface of the glycocalyx.&nbsp;<span id="ref34549323.30047864" class="cite">Trinh <em>et al.</em> 2018</span> reconstituted cholesterol transport by expressing the NTD as a fragment separate from the remaining portion of NPC1. When co-expressed, the two NPC1 fragments reconstitute cholesterol transport and showed that cholesterol can be transferred from the NTD of one full-length NPC1 to another NPC1 molecule that lacks the NTD. The locations of buried amino acids and docking studies have identified putative lipid binding domains that are in close proximity to amino acids that, when mutated, are connected to NPC1 loss-of-function (<span id="ref34549702.30942586" class="cite">Elghobashi-Meinhardt 2019</span>). Niemann-Pick type C 1 function requires lumenal domain residues that mediate cholesterol-dependent NPC2 binding, and lysosomal cholesterol egress requires both NPC1 and NPC2.&nbsp;<span id="ref34551786.32544384" class="cite">Qian <em>et al.</em> 2020</span> presented systematic structural characterizations that revealed the molecular basis for low-pH-dependent cholesterol delivery from NPC2 to the transmembrane domain of NPC1. At pH 8.0, similar structures of NPC1 were obtained in nanodiscs and in detergent. A tunnel connecting the N-terminal domain (NTD) and the transmembrane sterol-sensing domain (SSD) was unveiled. At pH 5.5, the NTD exhibits two conformations, suggesting the motion for cholesterol delivery to the tunnel. A cholesterol molecule was found at the membrane boundary of the tunnel, and TMS2 moves toward formation of a surface pocket on the SSD. The structure of the NPC1-NPC2 complex at 4.0 &Aring; resolution was obtained at pH 5.5, elucidating the molecular basis for cholesterol handoff from NPC2 to NPC1(NTD) (<span id="ref34551786.32544384" class="cite">Qian <em>et al.</em> 2020</span>). Genetic diversity in Niemann-Pick C1 can be managed through modulation of the Hsp70 chaperone system (<span id="ref34552494.31509197" class="cite">Wang <em>et al.</em> 2020</span>). The NPC1 protein is evolutionarily conserved with homologues reported in yeast to humans; NPC2 is present in <em>C. elegans</em> to humans. While neurons in mammalian models of NPC1 and NPC2 diseases exhibit many changes that are similar to those in humans (e.g., endosomal/lysosomal storage, Golgi fragmentation, neuroaxonal dystrophy, neurodegeneration), a reduced degree of ectopic dendritogenesis and an absence of neurofibrillary tangles (NFTs) in these species suggest important differences in the way lower mammalian neurons respond to NPC1/NPC2 loss of function (<span id="ref34552565.15465426" class="cite">Walkley and Suzuki 2004</span>). Cholesterol transport studies using wild-type NPC1 and the P691S mutant suggest changes in dynamical behavior as determined using molecular dynamics simulations  (<span id="ref34552653.32331453" class="cite">Elghobashi-Meinhardt 2020</span>). NPC1 mutations cause variable disease phenotypes (<span id="ref34553398.32248828" class="cite">Musalkova <em>et al.</em> 2020</span>). Filoviruses, including marburgviruses and ebolaviruses, have a single transmembrane glycoprotein (GP) that facilitates their entry into cells. During entry, GP needs to be cleaved by host proteases to expose the receptor-binding site that binds to the endosomal receptor Niemann-Pick C1 (NPC1) protein. Crystal structural analyses of the cleaved GP (GPcl) of Ebola virus (EBOV) in complex with human NPC1 has shown that NPC1 has two protruding loops (loops 1 and 2), which engage a hydrophobic pocket on the head of EBOV GPcl (<span id="ref34553702.34069246" class="cite">Igarashi <em>et al.</em> 2021</span>). Cholesterol docking studies, focusing on binding recognition, showed differences in the binding positions of mutant variants versus the wild-type protein (<span id="ref34553889.31506030" class="cite">Mart&iacute;nez-Archundia <em>et al.</em> 2020</span>). Commonalities between Niemann-Pick C1 disease and other lysosomal storage disorders have been reviewed (<span id="ref34554229.32522631" class="cite">Ya&ntilde;ez <em>et al.</em> 2020</span>). Cholesterol binding to the sterol-sensing region of the Niemann Pick C1 protein confines dynamics of its N-terminal domain (<span id="ref34554232.33021976" class="cite">Dubey <em>et al.</em> 2020</span>). Variants in the Niemann-Pick type C gene, NPC1, are probably not associated with Parkinson's disease (<span id="ref34554263.32371106" class="cite">Ouled Amar Bencheikh <em>et al.</em> 2020</span>). NPC1 exports low-density-lipoprotein (LDL)-derived cholesterol from lysosomes. TMSs 3-7 of NPC1 comprise the Sterol-Sensing Domain (SSD). The anti-fungal drug itraconazole abolishes NPC1 activity in cells.&nbsp;<span id="ref34554288.31919352" class="cite">Long <em>et al.</em> 2020</span> reported a cryo-EM structure of human NPC1 bound to itraconazole, which reveals how this binding site in the center of NPC1 blocks a putative lumenal tunnel linked to the SSD. Blocking this tunnel abolishes NPC1-mediated cholesterol egress. The palmitate anchor of Hedgehog occupies a similar site in the homologous tunnel of Patched, suggesting a conserved mechanism for sterol transport in this family of proteins and establishing a central function of their SSDs. Npc1, acting in neurons and glia, is essential for the formation and maintenance of CNS myelin (<span id="ref34554292.23593041" class="cite">Yu and Lieberman 2013</span>). In contrast to the benign Q92S mutation, Q92R reduces electrostatic potential around S-opening, and thus likely affects the NPC1 (NTD)-NPC2 interaction and/or cholesterol transfer from NPC2 to NPC1 (<span id="ref34554297.30183109" class="cite">Petukh and Zhulin 2018</span>). In the somatosensory neocortex, NPC1 plays a role in synaptic function (<span id="ref34554305.22325094" class="cite">Avchalumov <em>et al.</em> 2012</span>). Lysosomes receive extracellular and intracellular cholesterol and redistribute it throughout the cell. Cholesterol egress from lysosomes is critical for cholesterol homeostasis, and its failure underlies the pathogenesis of genetic disorders such as Niemann-Pick C disease. <span id="ref34554958.35653304" class="cite">Anderson <em>et al.</em> 2022</span> reported that the BORC-ARL8-HOPS ensemble is required for egress of free cholesterol from lysosomes and for storage of esterified cholesterol in lipid droplets. Depletion of BORC, ARL8 or HOPS does not alter the localization of the lysosomal transmembrane cholesterol transporter NPC1 to degradative compartments, but decreases the association of the luminal transporter NPC2 and increases NPC2 secretion. BORC-ARL8-HOPS depletion also increases lysosomal degradation of CI-MPR, which normally sorts NPC2 to the endosomal-lysosomal system and then is recycled to the trans-Golgi network (TGN). These defects likely result from impaired HOPS-dependent fusion of endosomal-lysosomal organelles and an uncharacterized function of HOPS in CI-MPR recycling. Thus, the BORC-ARL8-HOPS ensemble is required for cholesterol egress from lysosomes by enabling CI-MPR-dependent trafficking of NPC2 to the endosomal-lysosomal system (<span id="ref34554958.35653304" class="cite">Anderson <em>et al.</em> 2022</span>). Members of this familly have the NPC1 fold (<span id="ref34555317.36164651" class="cite">Ferrada and Superti-Furga 2022</span>). A synopsis of inborn errors in sphingolipid and cholesterol metabolism in the Niemann-Pick type diseases has appearred (<span id="ref34556201.37003582" class="cite">Pfrieger 2023</span>). Iron limitation restores autophagy and increases lifespan in the yeast model of Niemann-Pick type C1 (<span id="ref34556591.37047194" class="cite">Martins <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>).&nbsp; The NPCs play a key role in the prognosis and diagnosis of&nbsp;liver hepatocellular carcinoma (LIHC) and may 
be an important indicator for LIHC prognosis and diagnosis; thus, NPC1 might 
be a potential therapeutic target in LIHC (<span id="ref34557004.37541978" class="cite">Chen <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>). Species-specific differences in NPC1 protein trafficking govern therapeutic responses in Niemann-Pick type C disease (<span id="ref34557360.36301667" class="cite">Schultz <em>et al.</em> 2022</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Animals</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>NPC1 and NPC2 of <em>Homo sapiens</em> <br /> NPC1 (AAH63302) <br /> NPC2 (AAH02532)</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="2.A.6.6.10"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=2.A.6.6.10&tcname=">2.A.6.6.10</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Uncharacterized pacific oyster protein of 926 aas and 6 - 10 TMSs.&nbsp; It is of unknown function, but has a large C-terminal domain that resembles 3-hydroxy-3-methylglutaryl-coenzyme A reductase.</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Animals</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>UP of <em>Crassostrea angulata</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="2.A.6.6.11"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=2.A.6.6.11&tcname=">2.A.6.6.11</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>3-hydroxy-3-methylglutaryl (HMG)-coenzyme A reductase of 438 aas and 1 or 0 TMSs. This protein resembles other HMG-CoA reductases which have N-terminal TMSs that show sequence similarity to RND transporters.</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>3HMG-coenzyme A reductase of <em>Bdellovibrio bacteriovorus</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="2.A.6.6.12"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=2.A.6.6.12&tcname=">2.A.6.6.12</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p><span class="recommended-name">Niemann-Pick C1 protein, NPC1 of 1339 aas and 16 TMSs in a 1 + 3 + 1 +5 + 1 +5 arrangement. </span>Trafficking of EhNPC1 and EhNPC2 during cholesterol uptake and phagocytosis as well as their association with molecules involved in endocytosis clearly suggest that these proteins play a key role in cholesterol uptake (<span id="ref34546886.28002502" class="cite">Bola&ntilde;os <em>et al.</em> 2016</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>NPC1 of <em>Entamoeba histolytica</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="2.A.6.6.13"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=2.A.6.6.13&tcname=">2.A.6.6.13</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Patched, PTCH1, Ptc1 or PTCH, SLC65 family) of 1447 aas and 12 TMSs.&nbsp; RND transporter-like receptor, regulates the activity of Smoothened of 7 TMSs via the Hedgehog (HH) pathway. The Na<sup>+</sup> gradient may provide the energy for Patched activity (<span id="ref34547642.29229834" class="cite">Myers <em>et al.</em> 2017</span>). Possibly Patched1, chemiosmotically driven by the transmembrane Na<sup>+</sup> gradient, regulates Smoothened. 3-d structures have been solved at ~ 3.5 &Aring; resolution by cryoEM (<span id="ref34548919.30139912" class="cite">Qi <em>et al.</em> 2018</span>). This protein seems to have both transport and signalling functions, exporting cholesterol from the inner leaflet of the membrane out to the external environment. It displays a tunnel going through the membrane with a lateral opening, all large enough to accomodate cholesterol (<span id="ref34548919.30139912" class="cite">Qi <em>et al.</em> 2018</span>; <span id="ref34548920.30287647" class="cite">Sommer and Lemmon 2018</span>). The distrutribution of Smoothened on the primary cillium surface has been studied (<span id="ref34549371.30598282" class="cite">Yoon <em>et al.</em> 2019</span>).<br />Hedgehog protein signals mediate tissue patterning and maintenance by binding to and inactivating their common receptor Patched that suppresses the activity of the 7-TMS protein Smoothened. Loss of Patched function, the most common cause of basal cell carcinoma, permits unregulated activation of Smoothened and of the Hedgehog pathway. A cryo-EM structure of Patched revealed striking transmembrane domain similarities to prokaryotic RND transporters in whick a central hydrophobic conduit with cholesterol-like contents courses through the extracellular domain and resembles that used by other RND proteins to transport substrates, suggesting that Patched catalyzes cholesterol transport (<span id="ref34549122.30415841" class="cite">Zhang <em>et al.</em> 2018</span>). Cholesterol in the inner leaflet of the plasma membrane is reduced by PTCH1 expression but rapidly restored by Hedgehog stimulation, suggesting that PTCH1 regulates Smoothened by controlling cholesterol availability.<br />Key proteins in the Hedgehog-signalling pathway dynamically localize in primary cilia, antenna-like solitary organelles present on most cells. The secreted Hedgehog ligand Sonic Hedgehog (SHH) binds to its receptor Patched1 (PTCH1) in primary cilia, causing its inactivation and delocalization from cilia. At the same time, the transmembrane protein Smoothened (SMO or SMOH; TC# 9.A.14.16.4) is released of its inhibition by PTCH1 and accumulates in cilia. PTCH1 inactivation by SHH changes the diffusive motion of PTCH1 (<span id="ref34549608.30819883" class="cite">Weiss <em>et al.</em> 2019</span>). PTCH1 is mutated in the nevoid basal cell carcinoma syndrome (NBCCS) (<span id="ref34552427.33298892" class="cite">Nakase <em>et al.</em> 2020</span>).</p>
<p>Patched (Ptc1) acts as a suppressor for Hedgehog (Hh) signaling by depleting sterols in the cytoplasmic membrane leaflet that are required for the activation of downstream regulators. The positive modulator Hh inhibits Ptc1's transport function by binding to Ptc1 and its co-receptors, which are locally concentrated in invaginated microdomains known as caveolae.&nbsp;<span id="ref34553515.34174188" class="cite">Luo <em>et al.</em> 2021</span> reconstituted the mouse Ptc1 into lipid nanodiscs and determined its structure by cryoEM. The structure is&nbsp; similar to those in amphipol and detergents but displays various conformational differences in the transmembrane region. Although most particles are monomers, Ptc1 dimers were also observed with distinct interaction patterns and different membrane curvatures, some of which are reminiscent of caveolae. An extramembranous "hand-shake" region, rich in hydrophobic and aromatic residues, mediates inter-Ptc1 interactions under conditions of different membrane curvature, providing a plausible framework for Ptc1 clustering in the highly curved caveolae (<span id="ref34553515.34174188" class="cite">Luo <em>et al.</em> 2021</span>). Cholesterol regulates reception of the Hedgehog (Hh) signal in target cells. In vertebrates, cell-surface organelles called primary cilia function as compartments for the propagation of Hh signals. Various studies have led to the model that Patched-1 (PTCH1), the receptor for Hh ligands, uses its transporter-like activity to lower cholesterol accessibility in the membrane surrounding primary cilia (<span id="ref34553965.34562244" class="cite">Kinnebrew <em>et al.</em> 2022</span>). PTCH1 depletes accessible cholesterol in the outer leaflet of the membrane in a manner regulated by its ligand Sonic Hedgehog and the transmembrane potassium gradient.&nbsp;<span id="ref34554041.34698632" class="cite">Kinnebrew <em>et al.</em> 2021</span> proposed that PTCH1 moves cholesterol from the outer to the inner leaflet of the membrane in exchange for potassium ion export. The transition between 'inward-open' and solvent 'occluded' states is accompanied by Na<sup>+</sup> induced pinching of intracellular helical segments. Thus, the energetics and ion-coupling stoichiometries of PTCH1 transport mechanisms are revealed, whereby 1-3 Na<sup>+</sup> or 2-3 K<sup>+</sup> couple to cholesterol export, and provide a molecular description of transitions between distinct transport states (<span id="ref34556125.36824746" class="cite">Ansell <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>). PTCH1 inhibits the G protein-coupled receptor, Smoothened (SMO), via a debated mechanism involving modulation of ciliary cholesterol accessibility. There is an energetic barrier of ~15 to 20 kilojoules per mole for cholesterol export. The transition between "inward-open" and solvent "occluded" states is accompanied by Na<sup>+</sup>-induced pinching of intracellular helical segments. Thus, the findings illuminate the energetics and ion coupling stoichiometries of PTCH1 transport&nbsp; (<span id="ref34556125.36824746" class="cite">Ansell <em>et al.</em> 2023</span>).</p>

The cholesterol-sensing protein Scap induces cholesterol synthesis by transporting membrane-bound transcription factors called sterol regulatory element-binding proteins (SREBPs) from the theendoplasmic reticulum (ER) to the Golgi apparatus for proteolytic activation. Transport requires interaction between Scap's two ER luminal loops (L1 and L7), which flank an intramembrane sterol-sensing domain (SSD). Expression of yeast NP-C-related gene 1 (NCR1), the orthologue of the human NP-C gene 1 (NPC1) defective in the disease, in Chinese hamster ovary NPC1 mutant cells suppressed lipid accumulation (<span id="ref34553930.14970192" class="cite">Malathi <em>et al.</em> 2004</span>). <p>.&nbsp;</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Protein, yeast</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>NCR1/NPC2 of <em>Saccharomyces cerevisiae</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="2.A.6.6.4"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=2.A.6.6.4&tcname=">2.A.6.6.4</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>SREBP cleavage-activating protein, Scap of 1279 aas.&nbsp; Cholesterol homeostasis is mediated by Scap, a polytopic ER protein that transports SREBPs from ER
to Golgi where SREBPs are processed to forms that activate cholesterol synthesis. Scap has eight
transmembrane helices and two large luminal loops, designated Loop1 and Loop7. Evidence suggests that Loop1 binds to Loop7, allowing Scap to bind COPII proteins for transport in
coated vesicles (<span id="ref34545240.27068746" class="cite">Zhang <em>et al.</em> 2016</span>). When ER cholesterol rises, it binds to Loop1 causeing

complex to be secreted.&nbsp; Point mutations that disrupt the

The cryo-EM structure of human SCAP bound to Insig-2 suggests how their interaction is regulated by sterols (<span id="ref34552834.33446483" class="cite">Yan <em>et al.</em> 2021</span>). The sterol regulatory element-binding protein (SREBP) pathway controls

of the Scap-S4 segment is crucial for 25HC binding and Insig 
association (<span id="ref34552834.33446483" class="cite">Yan <em>et al.</em> 2021</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Animals</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>SCAP of <em>Cricetulus griseus </em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="2.A.6.6.5"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=2.A.6.6.5&tcname=">2.A.6.6.5</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>3-hydroxy-3-methylglutaryl (HMG)-CoA reductase of 888 aas and 6 - 10 TMSs.</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Animals</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>HMG-CoA reductase of <em>Homo sapiens</em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="2.A.6.6.6"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=2.A.6.6.6&tcname=">2.A.6.6.6</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p><a></a></p>
<p><!-- test--></p>
<p><a id="2.A.6.6.6"></a></p>
<p><a></a></p>
<p><!-- test--></p>
<p><a id="2.A.6.6.6"></a></p>
<p>Liver/intestinal enterocyte brush border Niemann-Pick C1 like 1 (NPC1L1; SLC65A2) protein; responsible for ezetimibe-sensitive absorption of luminal lipids and cholesterol&nbsp; as well as lutein via a transport mechanism (<a class="reflink" href="result.php?tc=2.A.6#ref2822">Altmann <em>et al</em>., 2004</a>; <a class="reflink" href="result.php?tc=2.A.6#ref3075">Davies <em>et al</em>., 2005</a>; <a class="reflink" href="result.php?tc=2.A.6#ref4435">Liscum, 2007</a>, <a class="reflink" href="result.php?tc=2.A.6#ref6583">Dixit <em>et al.</em> 2007</a>; <span id="ref34552519.24036530" class="cite">Reboul 2013</span>). NPC1L1-dependent sterol uptake seems to be a clathrin-mediated endocytic
 process and is regulated by cellular cholesterol content (<a class="reflink" href="result.php?tc=2.A.6#ref6987">Betters and Yu, 2010</a>; <a class="reflink" href="result.php?tc=2.A.6#ref7273">Jia <em>et al.</em>, 2011</a>).&nbsp; Dietary cholesterol induces trafficking of the intestinal NPC1L1 from the brush boarder to endosomes (<a class="reflink" href="result.php?tc=2.A.6#ref9285">Skov <em>et al.</em> 2011</a>).&nbsp; It distributes on the brush border membranes of 
enterocytes and the canalicular membranes of hepatocytes. It is the target of ezetimibe, a hypocholesterolemic drug which blocks internalization of NPC1L1 and cholesterol in the mouse small intestine (<a class="reflink" href="result.php?tc=2.A.6#ref9270">Wang and Song 2012; </a><a class="reflink" href="result.php?tc=2.A.6#ref9607">Xie <em>et al.</em> 2012</a>). Human <span class="highlight">NPC1L1</span> is a 1,332-amino acid protein with a putative sterol-sensing domain 
(SSD) that shows sequence homology to HMG-CoA reductase (HMGCR), 
Niemann-Pick C1 (NPC1), and SREBP cleavage-activating protein (SCAP). NPC1L1 may have evolved at two sites (apical
membrane of enterocytes and canalicular membrane of hepatocytes) to mediate cholesterol uptake
through a clathrin-mediated endocytic process, protecting the body against fecal and biliary loss of
cholesterol (<span id="ref34544897.18460917" class="cite">Yu 2008</span>). NPC1L1-dependent intestinal cholesterol absorption appears to require ganglioside GM3 in membrane microdomains (<span id="ref34550231.30242108" class="cite">Nihei <em>et al.</em> 2018</span>). Ezetimibe is the only inhibitor of NPC1L1 available for clinical use, but aminoss-lactam ezetimibe derivatives may also be effective (<span id="ref34552502.26923679" class="cite">Pirillo <em>et al.</em> 2016</span>). The cholesterol absorption inhibitor, ezetimibe, acts by blocking the sterol-induced internalization of NPC1L1 (<span id="ref34552544.18522832" class="cite">Ge <em>et al.</em> 2008</span>). Otopetrin 1 activation by purinergic nucleotides regulates intracellular calcium ions (<span id="ref34547903.17606897" class="cite">Hughes <em>et al.</em> 2007</span>). NPC1L1 plays a major role in the intestinal absorption of biliary cholesterol, vitamin E, and vitamin K. The drug ezetimibe inhibits NPC1L1-mediated absorption of cholesterol, lowering of circulating levels of low-density lipoprotein cholesterol. <span id="ref34553600.34407950" class="cite">Long <em>et al.</em> 2021 </span>reported cryoEM structures of human NPC1L1 bound to either cholesterol or a lipid resembling vitamin E. The same intramolecular channel in hNPC1L1 mediates transport of vitamin E and cholesterol. hNPC1L1 exists primarily as a homodimer; dimerization is mediated by aromatic residues within a region of TMS 2 that exhibits a horizonal orientation in the membrane. Mutation of tryptophan-347, lieing in this region, disrupts dimerization, and the resultant monomeric NPC1L1 exhibits reduced efficiency of cholesterol uptake (<span id="ref34553600.34407950" class="cite">Long <em>et al.</em> 2021</span>).</p>
<p>&nbsp;</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Animals</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>NPC1L1 of <em>Homo sapiens</em> (NP_037521)</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="2.A.6.6.7"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=2.A.6.6.7&tcname=">2.A.6.6.7</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Niemann-Pick C-type protein (NPC) (1342 aas; 16 putative TMSs in a 1+3+1+5+1+5 arrangement)</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Slime molds</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>NPC of <em>Dictyostelium discoideum </em>(Q9TVK6)&nbsp;</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="2.A.6.6.8"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=2.A.6.6.8&tcname=">2.A.6.6.8</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Niemann-Pick C1 protein homologue-1, Ncr1; contains a sterol sensing domain. Loss prevents hedgehog signaling. (<span id=""ref3918.15110707"" class=""cite"">Nakano <em>et al</em>., 2004</span>; <span id=""ref3922.17429392"" class=""cite"">Higgins, 2007</span>). The intracellular movement of this Hedgehog signal is complex, and involves insertion into the apical cell membrane, 
followed by endocytosis, and then secretion at the basolateral membrane 
by Dispatched (<span id=""ref34548312.29577985"" class=""cite"">Nikaido 2018</span>).
 Dispatched shows a typical RND protein folding pattern, although its 
overall sequence similarity to Patched or NPC1 
is low. However, it contains the typical sterol sensing domain,&nbsp; similar to those found in NPC1 as well as in Patched. Hedgehog anchored by glycosylphophatidylinositol, 
rather than cholesterol, was not exported by Dispatched, suggesting that
 the cholesterol anchor is recognized by the transporter.
 Finally, the secretion of Hedgehog by Dispatched across the membrane 
requires help by other proteins, including a secreted soluble protein 
called Scube (<span id=""ref34548312.29577985"" class=""cite"">Nikaido 2018</span>).</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Animals</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Dispatched of <em>Drosophila melanogaster</em> (AAF_23397)</p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#99ccff"><TD><b><A NAME="2.A.6.9.2"></A><A HREF="tcprotein.php?substrate=2.A.6.9.2&tcname=">2.A.6.9.2</a></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p><a></a></p>
<p><!-- test--></p>
<p>Protein dispatched homologue 1 (Dispatched-1, DispA, DISP1) of 1521 aas and 12 TMSs.&nbsp; The human ortholog (Q96F81, 1524 aas) is 83% identical.&nbsp; They play roles in congenital diaphragmatic hernia (CDH) and associated pulmonary hypoplasia (PH) in humans and mice (<span id="ref34549235.30382378" class="cite">Takahashi <em>et al.</em> 2018</span>). Hedgehog (HH) signaling is essential for metazoan development. The HH ligand is secreted into the extracellular space by DISP1. <span id="ref34553761.32646883" class="cite">Chen <em>et al.</em> 2020</span> reported the cryo-EM structure of human DISP1. It contains 12 TMSs and two extracellular domains (ECDs) like other RND homologs. Its ECDs reveal an open state, in contrast to its structural homologues PTCH1 and NPC1, whose extracellular/luminal domains adopt a closed state. The low-resolution structure of the DISP1 complex with dual lipid-modified HH ligand reveals how the ECDs of DISP1 engage with HH ligand. Several cholesterol-like molecules were found in the TMSs, implying a transport-like function of DISP1 (<span id="ref34553761.32646883" class="cite">Chen <em>et al.</em> 2020</span>). Dispatched enables tissue-patterning activity of the lipid-modified Hedgehog protein by releasing it from tightly -localized sites of embryonic expression. <span id="ref34554012.34707294" class="cite">Wang <em>et al.</em> 2021</span> determined a cryoEM structure of the mouse DISP1, revealing three Na<sup>+</sup> ions coordinated within a channel that traverses its transmembrane domain. The rate of Hedgehog export is dependent on the Na<sup>+</sup> gradient across the plasma membrane. The transmembrane channel and Na<sup>+</sup> binding are disrupted in DISP1-NNN, a variant with asparagine substitutions for three intramembrane aspartate residues that each coordinate and neutralize the charge of one of the three Na<sup>+</sup> ions. DISP1-NNN and variants that disrupt single Na<sup>+</sup> sites retain binding to, but are impaired in export of the lipid-modified Hedgehog protein to the SCUBE2 acceptor. Interaction of the amino-terminal signalling domain of the Sonic hedgehog protein (ShhN) with DISP1 occurs via an extensive buried surface area and contacts with an extended furin-cleaved DISP1 arm. Variability analysis reveals that ShhN binding is restricted to one extreme of a continuous series of DISP1 conformations. The bound and unbound DISP1 conformations display distinct Na<sup>+</sup>-site occupancies, which suggests a mechanism by which transmembrane Na<sup>+</sup> flux may power extraction of the lipid-linked Hedgehog signal from the membrane. Na<sup>+</sup>-coordinating residues in DISP1 are conserved in PTCH1 and other metazoan RND family members, suggesting that Na<sup>+</sup> flux powers their conformationally driven activities (<span id="ref34554012.34707294" class="cite">Wang <em>et al.</em> 2021</span>). The substrate is probably the sonic hedgehog (SHH) protein of 462 aas and one N-terminal TMS.</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Animals</p></TD><TD><b><font color=#3333CC><p>Disp1 of <em>Mus musculus </em></p></TD></TR><TR bgcolor="#ffffff" valign=top><TD COLSPAN=4 valign=top>&nbsp;</CENTER></TD></TR></TABLE>
